The author of this book says: When I had completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of the science of medicine in its completeness, and had reached the utmost point in it of clarity and explanation, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the work of the hand.
Because the work of the hand is entirely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its science has almost been forgotten and its practice has ceased. And there remain only a few traces of it in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by error and confusion, so that its meaning has become obscure and its usefulness has been diminished.
Therefore I saw fit to revive it and to compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this adds to the clarity and is a great help to what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. Until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, their number, origins, and the pulsating and non-pulsating vessels, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery. We have mentioned some of this in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned of anatomy will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have often seen among those who have imagined themselves knowledgeable in this science and claimed it without knowledge or training.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a pig-like swelling in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who was suffering from a fistula, and the stone was large. He acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder's substance. And the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it; I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that I could have estimated it.
And I saw another physician who earned his living from some of the brothel keepers of our city through medicine.
Then a black boy came to him who had a fracture in his leg near the heel, together with a wound. The physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and did not leave the wound any space to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for some days, and ordered him not to unbind the bandages until his leg and foot swelled and he was close to death.
Then I was called to him, and I hastened to unbind the bandages, so he found relief and recovered from his pains, except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after several days, until the misfortune of its owner increased.  That is because if the cancer is purely of black humor, then it should not be treated with iron at all.
Except that it be in a member which can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place that comes in this book about the operation which contains danger and fear, so you should avoid it and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to speak and criticize, therefore take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases which are difficult to cure.
And purify yourselves from that which you fear might cast suspicion upon your religion and your worldly affairs; for this is more enduring for your reputation and higher in this world and the next for your status.
For Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This chapter is divided into three sections.
The first section.
Concerning cauterization with fire and cauterization with strong medicine, arranged from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
Concerning incision, puncturing, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged in chapters, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
Concerning setting, reduction, and the treatment of fractures and the like, arranged in chapters from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
Concerning cautery.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary that we mention the manner of its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that the discussion concerning the manner of the benefit of cautery and its harms is a long discussion, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken of it.
And they differed concerning it, and I have shortened from their speech a little, for fear of lengthening.
And I say that cautery, generally speaking, benefits every bad temperament that is with matter and without matter, except for two temperaments, and they are the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed concerning it.  Some of them said that cautery is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cautery is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured from a hot dry disease with a hot dry medicine. And he who says the opposite said that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the body of man cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not form an opinion on that matter except one who has practiced and trained in the art of cautery to a great extent. And he has observed the differences in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially the cold and moist diseases.  All the physicians have agreed on them and have not differed in the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, O my sons, that among the secrets of treatment is cautery with fire, and its advantage over cautery with a burning medicine, because fire is a simple element whose action does not extend beyond the member which is cauterized, nor does it harm another member connected to it except a little harm.
And cautery with a burning medicine may extend its action to what is beyond the members, and perhaps it causes in the member a disease difficult to treat, and perhaps it kills.  Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one is excessive.
And this has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding of the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy discussion.  Were it not that it is not fitting for this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its removal of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative speech too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cautery, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cautery is suitable at any time, because the harm resulting from the time is consumed by the benefit which is brought about by cautery. Especially if the cautery is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, because of what is feared from them that a calamity will follow which is greater than the slight harm incurred from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cautery which cures a certain disease will never recur, and that you should make it a necessary procedure.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cautery is only like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cautery is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its effect.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength and hold of his disease, and what is prepared in his body from the gathering of humors in it, and his neglect of himself in acquiring them from foods and the like of causes.
Except that the disease in which cautery is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like; it is possible that that pain may not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cautery is the last of medicine, it is a true saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment benefits with medicine or otherwise after the occurrence of cautery, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cautery being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cautery as the last thing, and it works; from here it came to pass that cautery is the last of medicine. Not in the sense to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate; and that is not absolutely the case.
Because I have tried that and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and superior to iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire, made of gold, you cannot determine when it is heated to the degree you want, due to the redness of the gold.
And because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat on it, it melts in the fire and flows, so the craftsman is troubled by that; therefore, cauterization with iron is quicker and closer to the truth for us to work with, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections which I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student what he wants from it, God willing.
The first chapter.
Cauterization of the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the predominance of moisture and cold on the brain, which are the cause of headache and frequent colds from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain. And in short, for every illness that occurs from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habit of the patient.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your hand on the root of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, which is of this form.
Expose the bone to the extent of the head of a toothpick or the size of a carob seed; then lift your hand, otherwise replace your hand with the same or another instrument if it has cooled. Until you see of the bone what I mentioned to you; then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a cotton ball in it, place it on the spot, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton ball soaked in fat over it and leave it until the scab from the cautery is gone; then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus is discharged, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what is burned from the bone afterward, then treating it.
And others said that one should exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, so that it falls from the bone in the form of a carat or a small flake; and they claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that place, and the wound is left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and by way of risk; and leaving it, in my opinion, is better and safer.  For if there is [a problem], the head weakens whenever its natural connection is disrupted, as we have seen in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it; therefore, act according to it and be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and persists in the patient, and applications, collyria, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if he has been cauterized with the single cautery that we described, and nothing of that has benefited him, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cautery. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone that we described is uncovered. And cauterize him at the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient will find this very painful, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head; and I will mention this cautery in its place.
The cautery with which the temples and the back of the head are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized.  And this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head, with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment that I have mentioned in the classifications of diseases.  If that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide lancet until there is a space under the skin large enough for the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply a cotton swab soaked in butter until the place suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  And if you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines which I mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery iron, whose image is this:
It is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, with some perforation and a small projection in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. And lift your hand so that you do not burn the artery beneath, causing bleeding. Then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with ointment and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this, especially chronic, migraine.
Chapter Four.
Cauterization of chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with the aforementioned treatments and what we mentioned in the classifications of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see a cause for which the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is not sufficient. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it glows white, after you have marked the painful area with a line the length of half a finger or so, and you should move your hand once while pulling it so that you cut the artery and reach towards the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the jaw which moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it, which would cause spasm.
And be cautious and watchful of bleeding from the artery that you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he is doing and is not a skilled and experienced practitioner;  it is better to leave the procedure undone.
And the treatment of bleeding that occurs from an artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing; if you see a condition that this cauterization does not address.
And you see that the patient's body can tolerate it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery that we mentioned in the chapter on artery ligation with the double-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold, and it is treated with purgatives and other treatments that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it around the entire ear, as it goes around, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little away from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink. The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of Paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cauterization is that type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
And one should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and nervous tension.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with ointments, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has been ineffective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the base of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, for the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
And the image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cautery, and it is a type of scalpel, the image of which we have presented earlier, but it is a little gentler than it, as you see. The knife should have a little excess thickness. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of the chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned and your treatment is not effective and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize it four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head as previously described.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  It may also be cauterized on the stomach's opening, which will be more effective, then treat it as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is caused by phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and pills that cleanse the brain.
Then his entire head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a type of cauterization.
Do this in exactly the order I have described there. If he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, cauterize him three times on the back of his head, in a line from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the space between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize in the middle as well.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize on the temples, then treat until he is cured.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
Cauterization for paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you first proceed to purify the head with the purgatives and what we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in the case of relaxation of the body, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and intractable, then cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. And reach with the cautery until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand and then treat him as previously mentioned until he heals, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
Cauterization for Epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  It is necessary first to cleanse his brain with strong purgatives and with all the treatment that we have mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and can tolerate taking medicine.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicine, then let him use gargles and masticatories that cleanse the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then his head is shaved, and then one cauterization is performed in the middle of the head as previously described, and another cauterization at its back. And one cauterization on each temple of his head. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then perform the cauterizations that were mentioned for the patient with paralysis and body relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back, and the cautery should be olive-shaped as previously described.
If the patient is a child, then make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then perform the cauterizations that we mentioned for the patient with paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined toward black bile and the patient's body is moist, then purge him with that which cleanses his brain, as has been explained in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a firm cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head while the patient sits cross-legged holding it from each side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a fire with moderate heat, to the extent that the finger can tolerate it when inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week, along with all other good treatment, until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures, without holding your hand on the cautery, but rather by sprinkling; for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton wool compress soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing the descending water in the eye.
If the descending water in the eye becomes apparent to you through the signs I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly and purge the patient of whatever pollutes his head, and cleanse him of all humors, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and apply a cautery to the middle of the head, then apply two cauteries to the temples if the descent of water began in both eyes, or to one side if it was in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the width of the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see any of it, cut it immediately with whatever treatment is possible, and we will address the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting the arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And it may be cauterized at the nape of the neck under the two bones with two deep cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, outside the head, and you are certain that it is from cold, thick, phlegmatic excess, then cauterize it with the cauterization described earlier for the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head and two cauterizations on the temples and two cauterizations at the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need more, then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the corner of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective, then quickly purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle cauterization with the olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron two cauterizations above the eyebrows, a little below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it.  And this is the image of the nail-shaped [cautery iron].
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes from illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, and keep them distant from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be along the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate your hand with the cautery, but only to the extent that a third of the skin burns, and the shape of the cautery iron is like this.
Chapter sixteen.
In cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins turn inward so that it pricks the eye, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If it pricks him while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.  Then when they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an Asiatic leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins, then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron; this is its shape.
Then cauterize according to the shape you marked, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the Asiatic leaf, is entirely burned, especially its surface.  A sign of the correctness of your work is that you see the eyelid contract and the hair rise from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, then apply cotton with butter to it until the eschar separates. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization on that place as you did at first. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and the hair does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is that you instruct the patient to leave the margins until they grow long and become even.
Then make from paper a shape like an Asiatic leaf. Then take of the usual soap and of unslaked lime, of each one a drachm in weight or thereabouts, and crush them both together very finely. Do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper which you made in the shape of an Asiatic leaf, and place it on one or both eyelids. Place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head in your lap.
And place your index finger above the medicine and hold it lightly, moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And observe; if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting, otherwise repeat the application of the medicine to the place where the medicine did not take effect and did not become black, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized. Then apply cotton with butter to it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or the like until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the procedure to be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after some days and there is some fear of hair in the eye,
then reapply the medicine to that place of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did before, then treat it until it heals. This is the image of an Asiatic leaf.
And know that people's eyes differ in size, so let your cauterization be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from one who is experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
Concerning the cauterization of the canthus which appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the fistula with what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
You order the patient to place his head in your lap, and a servant holds his head in front of you, holding it still so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then you place a cotton ball moistened with egg white or with the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then you heat the cautery iron, which is of this form.
It is hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather from one end, which is the end used for cauterization; and if you wish, it may be perforated at the other end, and if you wish it may be solid like a bodkin, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then you squeeze the fistula if it is open and remove the matter from it and dry it; or if it is not open, you open it and remove its pus. Then you apply the cautery iron to it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
And move your hand slightly away during cauterization, from the eye towards the nose, lest your hand slip or the patient move, and the cautery iron fall into the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, reapply the cautery iron a second time if you need to.
And leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter to it and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it according to what will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients: you approach the location of the fistula, incise it, then place in the incision itself a thin cone, this is its image.
And you pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand firmly on the cone, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye. And you should place on the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or water.
For the lead burns the location of the fistula and heals it wonderfully. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment we have mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal perforation and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be described in its specific place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a hair, especially in the lips of boys; it occurs frequently. If you treat this cleft with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this shape.
Its cavity should be as fine as a knife. Then quickly apply it, hot, to the cleft itself, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft, then treat it with the qiryūṭī until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a canker sore occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the flow of pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the hole of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment, until it heals, God willing.
If the material is cut off and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On cauterizing loose molars and gums.
If the gums are relaxed from moisture and the molars become loose, and you have treated them with medicine but it has not worked, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you place the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then return the cautery iron several times, as much as you want, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And let him hold it for an hour and then spit it out; for the loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gum will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medicine has not worked, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, you take cow's butter and melt it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the melted butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth. And if you wish, you may dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick in its body so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth, then heat the cautery iron which is of that shape and place it on the tooth itself and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools. Do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away either that same day or the next. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and as is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing the gums.
If the gums are due to phlegm and cold humors and do not yield to ripening by medication, and you desire rapid ripening, then heat a hollow cautery iron, which has this form, perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits at the other end during cauterization, place it carefully on the tumor itself once or twice, if necessary, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it until what was burnt by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail over the windpipe, especially if this is accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery in the hollow of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower position.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then cauterize another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra; and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, cauterize two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped, in the manner previously described. And if you wish, let your cautery be by puncturing with the cautery called the point, the image of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache, and let the punctures be from thirty points or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers.  You may also make a cautery with three branches in this form, and it expedites the cauterization because you cauterize three cauteries at once with it. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain fixed when it is reduced from its dislocation so that this becomes a habit for it to return and then dislocate at the slightest movement exposed, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on the healthy side, and then the skin inside the armpit is lifted upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two heads, whose image is this.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization will be four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads, in which case the shape of the cauterization will then be six cauterizations, and the heads should be of moderate size.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And this number may be increased by one, making eight cauterizations. Then, apply finely ground balls mixed with salt to the cauterization. Then, the patient must remain still and not move the limb for a while until it strengthens.  And if the dislocation is upwards, which is seldom the case.
Cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-shaped cauterization or many cauterizations in a dotted pattern; for the joint will strengthen, the moisture will leave, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If coldness and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, deranging its temperament, and if many catarrhal discharges occur in it, and if it is treated with various remedies without success, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs and arms extended, and then three cauterizations should be performed. One cauterization should be performed below the pit of the stomach, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery iron; and two cauterizations lower down, on either side of the first, so that the cauterizations form a triangle, and farther apart so that they do not join if they suppurate.  The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery iron with which you perform this single cauterization should be circular, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing, for whoever is afraid of this cauterization. This is done by marking points on the stomach with ink to the desired extent, then cauterizing them with a punctate cautery iron, then treating it with the treatment previously described until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the cold cauterization of the liver.
If pain arises in the liver from coldness and dampness, or from a subtle wind, so that it has departed excessively from its natural temperament, and the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the classification, but this has not been effective. Then the patient should lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this form and this exact amount on the liver below the ribs, where the human elbow ends.
And after each cauterization, a cauterization should be made of the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be straight along the body; and do not press down hard on the cautery iron.
And let the amount of skin thickness burned be half, no more. And let the patient stand on his feet; if he is not able to stand, let him lie down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery iron.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with a knife-shaped cautery iron if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be careful lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will feel heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that the physicians have been exhausted in treating it, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a spatula, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in the art of medicine has been long and who has repeatedly experienced these diseases; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for cold fistulas in this manner: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and let it be about the thickness of a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cautery between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauteries a little below the jugular veins, slanting towards the side under the chin, and two large cauteries above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauteries also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slanting slightly backwards, and another cautery in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauteries on the back: one between the shoulders and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cautery which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be superficially on the skin.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used an iron cautery like a file, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the pus comes out as we mentioned in the tumor of the liver.  In this method, cauterization is dangerous; either the patient dies immediately or a fistula forms in the place which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to cauterize it three or four times, in a row, along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the cauterizations of the liver, whose shape we have presented, and between each cauterization and cauterization is the width of a finger or a little more. The nature of the cautery is the same as we mentioned in the cauterization of the liver, and do not deepen your hand with the cauterization. The patient’s position is lying on his back.
The other method is in the elbow, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends, and your lifting of the skin is across the width of the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then you insert two cauteries, very carefully protected, until you pass them through the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery so that the cauterizations are four. And if you wish, you may cauterize with the other cautery with three points.
Then you treat the place of cauterization after you leave it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned before.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in particular for ascitic dropsy; if you treat the dropsical patient with the various treatments that we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, and a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to gather the humors for him, so that healing may hasten to him.  The shape of the cautery with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its form has preceded. That with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the top of the foot in the hollow between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will ooze out, and the cautery is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization is done with the sharp end of the cautery; and the cauterization is along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that towards the middle of the leg; and on each thigh, two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time so that the water may ooze out, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter thirty-three.
Concerning the cauterization of diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, to the point of weakening the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see the patient able to withstand cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was previously explained in the cauterization of the stomach with the circular cautery iron and four cauterizations around the navel with a gentle, nail-shaped cautery iron, and a large cauterization, or two, on the coccyx.
If you see that the dampness is abundant and the patient is able to withstand it, then cauterize him on the pubis and on each flank, and perhaps you can add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one, for it is a successful treatment whose benefit is not missed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are of thick, cold humors or corrupt dampness, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the section, but the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, slightly below the coccyx, in a triangular shape.
A cauterization below the navel, about two fingers' width; and if you estimate that his stomach has become cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as mentioned before, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery iron; and leave the cauterization open for a time, then treat him until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, then heat the cautery iron which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the same cut wart, and deepen your hand until the cautery iron reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, then cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with whatever ointments suit them until they heal; and if you cauterize a large area with cotton wool also, it will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its vicinity.
If the patient does not consent to the incision and the procedure which we described in its place, and shrinks from that, then perhaps he will heal by cauterization.  If a fistula occurs in one place, and pus and corrupt humors flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is to measure it with a thin probe.
Then ascertain its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and to the depth which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt bodies are burnt, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And beware of burning a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein, if the fistula leads to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine.
Guard against all these places. You do this only if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you judge that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place closes and the matter ceases from it and remains so for a time, then know that it has completely healed.
And if the matter does not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a decayed bone or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a strong wind, and because of that the patient's strength diminishes, then it is necessary to cauterize it on the loins, over the kidneys themselves. Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery which was previously mentioned.  And perhaps you should cauterize it a third time in the same session. Then three cauterizations in a row would be most effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel, over the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to its left.
The distance after cauterization on each side should be the width of a thumb joint. And cauterize with one cauterization in the lower back, or two if needed. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cauterizing the womb.
If a substance from cold and dampness occurs in the womb, and because of that it is prevented from conception, and its discharge is spoiled, and its menstruation is difficult, or pain occurs to it upon its arrival, then it is necessary to cauterize three cauterizations around the navel, as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. And one cauterization on cotton in the lower back, or two, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous moist substances may settle in the hip joint, and be a cause for its dislocation.  Its sign is that the leg is longer than the other if one compares them to each other, and you will find emptiness in the place of the dislocation.  Then it is necessary to cauterize the patient on the hip joint itself, a semi-circular cauterization.
After you have drawn with ink around the joint, as it rotates so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle. The cautery, whose image is presented in cauterizing the stomach, should be used. If this cautery is not available to you, then cauterize with three cauterizations using the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization as deep as the entire thickness of the skin, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
In cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint and its cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the division, and the treatment has not been effective, and it has lasted for a long time, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with the foul-smelling bean or the prickly pear bean, or something similar.
Then shave it and apply the cautery in two ways: either cautery with burning medicines or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire is done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize on the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  One may also cauterize a single cauterization in the middle, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations. If you wish, shave it in the circle previously mentioned in the cauterization of hip dislocation, a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the description of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two cauterizations on the thigh at the place where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the malleolus, four fingers towards the lateral side slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to cauterize to the point of burning a nerve or a large artery, lest you thereby cause the patient a bad or lasting harm.
And I have seen one and another who was cauterized above the malleolus, and the cauterization was excessive, so the leg became gangrenous until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the whole leg was corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned concerning the cauterization of the hip a cauterization of this description:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter being half a span, and its mouth the thickness of a date pit or slightly less, and inside this cup another cup, and a third cup.
And the distance between each cup will be the width of a thumb's joint, and the cups will be open on both sides, and their height will be about one or two finger joints, and a handle of well-secured iron is made for them. And this is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient leaning on the sound side, and three round cauterizations are made at once, then it is left for three days and dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for several days.
Many, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The author of this book said: We have rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because of the few whom we find who can endure it, except that it is among the good cauterizations for those who endure it and its application is appropriate.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is that you make two cups similar to the rings through which the pulp of the pomegranate passes, of copper or iron.
Their rims will have a height of about two finger joints or so, and the distance between them will be the thickness of a finger, and they will be open at the bottom and open at the top, and some of them will be held together in this manner.
Then you lower them onto the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and you firmly hold your hand, then you pour between the two circles some sharp water, which is slightly warmed by the fire, and hold it for the duration of an hourglass, and the patient endures its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the burning subsides, then you raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the sharp water from the entire hip with fresh water, then you leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and you leave it for days so that pus flows from it, for that is more effective.
Then you treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, you make a mold for it that holds water, in the same way you made it for the hip. This is the description of the sharp water, and I have described it in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of alkali salt and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and put them in a new pot that has a single small hole in its bottom, just large enough for a gimlet to enter.  Place another glazed pot under the bottom of the pot.
And pour enough fresh water onto the alkali and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after having firmly pressed them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water drips into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water, and pour it onto more fresh lime and alkali.
Then it will be extremely sharp, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing various limbs, because it acts just like fire.  There are also other medications that cauterize the hip, such as thapsia, honey of spurge, and lime with soap, mixed together.
Galen claimed, recounting a story from the ancients, a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly emphasizing its importance, and claiming that it requires no other treatment and cures in a single application.
To the point that one might enter the bath carried and leave cured. This is done by taking green borage—if green is not available, take fresh dry borage—finely grinding it with some fat, and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg, or to the thigh.
It is bandaged and left for three hours, or however long the patient feels the burning subsides. Then he is put in the bath. When his body is damp, he is put in the tub. Then, God willing, the pain will leave and he will be cured. If not cured, repeat the application after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that one should not cauterize with these medications except after purging the body. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization is done in this manner: wool is taken and soaked in old oil and placed on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes. Then take another, and continue doing this until sensation reaches from the middle of the arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing.  This type of cauterization is called goat-hair cauterization.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning cauterization of back pain.
Pain in the back may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It is caused by the effusion of a cold, moist substance, and cauterization is only performed in this type alone.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance, then after the patient has vomited with the use of *ḥibb al-munta*n and the like, he should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the location with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see the patient can tolerate and his strength.
And the cauterization should be with a needle cautery; and if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail cautery in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment often affects young children, and its initial sign in a child is that he experiences shortness of breath upon standing and movement, and you find at the end of his spinal vertebrae a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see that and want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery in this manner. Let the cautery take on each side of the vertebra evenly; and if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a punctate cautery in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another.  Then treat the area until it heals as we have mentioned.
And take care not to use cautery in a hump that is from a spasm of the nerve.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If the joint pains are from cold humors that descend to any member of the body, then if the pains occur in the legs, it is customary for physicians to call that gout especially.
If the gout, whose cause is cold, is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and the pains do not go away, then cautery removes them.  This is to cauterize it, after evacuation, around the joint of the legs with many cauterizations. The cautery iron should be medium-sized and olive-shaped, in this manner.
And if you need to punctuate the surface of the leg, do so with a punctate cautery iron. If the pains ascend to the knees or to the other joints—and this often happens—then cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery iron.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your hand with the cautery; rather, it should be only to the thickness of the skin. If the pains ascend to the hips or to the back, then use what we mentioned concerning cautery in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, punctuate around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, punctuate each joint with a point and the metacarpus. If the pains ascend after days to the elbows or to the shoulders,
cauterize them on each side.  The patient should not be without good management and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and evacuates the phlegm, he will recover with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestines and fat descend to the testicle, and this is recent and early, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives which will move the stool from his bowels.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should order him to strain himself until the fat or intestines protrude, then you should return it with your finger.  Then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then let an assistant place his hand on the spot lest the intestine protrude. And you have spread the patient's legs apart and placed a cushion under him, and another assistant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, your hand holding the cautery upright and steady, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it another time if it does not reach the bone the first time.
And take care to prevent the intestine from protruding while you cauterize, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed.  And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with fat, until the scab from the fire is gone. Then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound closes.
And you should make his diet during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. And he should reduce exertion, overeating and drinking, and loud shouting.
For if this treatment is used in this way, he will be completely healed, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is recent, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize the hernia with a round cautery of its size. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing.  Some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making a horizontal line at the upper side of the hernia and another line downwards, and you place a single, nail-like cautery in the middle. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some part of the body due to a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery. The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, smallness, weakness and strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, that is good; otherwise repeat the cauterization.  For it is the nature of these pains to spread from one member to what is near it, therefore you should follow it with cauterization until the patient recovers.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy which is caused by corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, consider: if the leprosy is incipient and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not abated nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will overwhelm the patient's entire constitution, then cauterize the head with five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head as is customary, the second below it towards the forehead at the hairline, and two on the temples, and one at the back on the hollow of the nape.  And exert yourself a little with the cautery until it slightly affects the bone.
And scales will be removed from it, so that the thick vapors may more easily escape. Cauterize also the spleen in the same manner as before. But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and become clearly manifest, then it is necessary to cauterize with these cauterizations which we have mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, and two cauterizations on the cheeks, and two cauterizations on the cervical vertebrae, and six on the dorsal vertebrae, and a large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine, and another above it in the same area, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the breasts, and cauterize each joint of the fingers and toes with one cauterization, and each heel of his feet and the forearms.
And be careful of the nerve which is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them.  And a cauterization may be done on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you do, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of cauterization as the healthy person does, because his body is numb. And the cauteries should be large and small according to the members and joints, as previously described in the descriptions of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with crushed acorns mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: when a limb becomes numb and is treated with medicines, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, then cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to what the size of the limb deserves, whether large or small. And let your cauterization penetrate a little into the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And it may be that some numbness that occurs in the hand and foot in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb is cauterized, and the numbness will go away.
And let no one undertake this except one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo becomes chronic and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the skin's thickness, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, lamb's tongue, and pigeon's blood or partridge's blood—one part of each. Mix everything, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows. Some wise men have mentioned cauterizing it intensely in its center, but I do not recommend this, because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed this many times.
It is correct to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If a hemorrhoid develops and is slow to mature, either because of the causative factor, or because of the patient's age if he is elderly and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoid, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, puncturing it.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to cauterize it, take the cautery whose image is this.
And insert it into the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the pus may easily flow, then treat it as it should be treated until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the chancre.
A chancre is nothing but a corruption that spreads in the member and eats it, just as fire eats dry wood. If you see a chancre in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, take many small and large, nail-like cauteries, according to what suits that place where the chancre is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains at all. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that grow flesh. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthy flesh without corruption, otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas. And you may treat the chancre with caustic medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with caustic medicine in the classification, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with caustic water.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron, to the extent that it surrounds the nail from every side, and let its edge be thin. Then you lower it, hot, onto the nail, and then turn your hand with the iron around the nail until the iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it three days, it will suppurate, then you dress it with pounded wild lettuce mixed with salt and leave the dressing on it overnight; then it will be extracted from its roots.  Then treat the wound site with a flesh-generating ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
This frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  It is necessary that you take a tube of copper or iron or an eagle's feather and lower it onto the nail or wart, then pour a little caustic water into the tube.
And hold your hand as you turn it, slightly pricking with your hand so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the caustic water finds a way to penetrate to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the caustic water for a short time.
Then leave it, for the nail will be extracted by its roots. Do this one by one until you have treated all of them on the body. Then treat their locations after they are extracted with ointments that grow flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterization with a cautery.
If someone suffers from a convulsion due to a cold in the nerve, or from a quartan fever or something else, then you should cauterize him four or five times on the vertebrae of the back, with one cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on his chest. And on his stomach a cauterization with the olive-shaped cautery iron, for the convulsion will cease and the cold illness will quickly mature.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing boils that occur in the body.
Ugly boils may erupt in the body, arising from cold, thick, corrupt substances.  At their first appearance, each boil should be gently cauterized on its head with a reed whose tip has been ignited in the fire, or with a long cautery iron, or with a lentil-shaped cautery iron.
And abscesses may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner; the active cause of the ailment will not increase and will dissipate, and the patient will recover, except that this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing the bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Often bleeding from a severed artery occurs from an external wound, or from the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and the like, making it difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press firmly to contain the blood beneath your finger so that nothing escapes. Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has burst, and apply the cautery to the same vessel after quickly removing your finger, holding the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, upon removing your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire and continue to do this, one after another, until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, lest you cause further harm to the patient. Know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. For if it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it may be tightly bound with threads; or medications that stop bleeding may be applied, and the wound tightly bound with bandages.
But whoever tries to stop it with a ligature, or by tightening with rags, or by applying cauterizing agents, and so on, will not succeed in stopping it except rarely.  If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not available, he should quickly place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press very hard until the bleeding is contained and flows from above the wound and onto the artery.  The finger should not be removed, and it should be constantly cooled with very cold water until the blood clots, thickens, and stops.  During this time, consider what is needed, whether cauterization or medication.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter, we mentioned every disease for which cauterization with fire and cauterizing medication are suitable, along with their causes, reasons, instruments, the forms of cauteries, and we made that into chapters, from head to foot.
And I follow the same path in this chapter, so that the student may easily find what he seeks.
Before I begin, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more pitfalls than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, caution should be greater in this chapter, because the work in this chapter frequently involves the loss of blood which sustains life, when opening a vein, or incising a tumor, or lancing an abscess, or treating a wound, or removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like; all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them death occurs.
