And I advise you to avoid that which is dubious, for various kinds of people may come to you in this profession with various kinds of illnesses; some of whom may be weary of their illness and death has become easy for them.
Because of the severity of their illness, and the length of their affliction, and in the illness is that which indicates death.  And some of them will give you their wealth and enrich you with it hoping for health, and their illness is deadly; therefore you should absolutely not assist anyone of this description who comes to you.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything of this unless after certain knowledge that is confirmed for you as to what the favorable outcome will be.  And in all the treatment of your patients, use foreknowledge and warning as to what leads to safety.
For in that you will have aid in acquiring praise, glory, mention and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and may He not deprive you of correctness and success; indeed that is in His hand, there is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter in sections, as preceded in the chapter on cautery, from head to foot, so that what you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
The first chapter.
In the treatment of the water which collects in the heads of infants.
This ailment frequently affects infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone except infants, and all those I have seen of them have died quickly; therefore I have seen it abandoned. And I have seen an infant whose head was full of water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the infant could not sit up because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or it gathers under the bone on the dura mater.  The procedure in this, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that one should make a single transverse incision in the middle of the head, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, so that the moisture flows out. This is the image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone—and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side and the water sinks down if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from you—then it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with cloths and bandages, then you anoint it from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day, then you loosen the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments, and do not leave off binding the head moderately, and nourish the patient with every dry food with little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the gathering of water appear, for it may be in the back of the head more, or in its front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim with the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appeared to you, so you incise it as you can, and be careful not to cut a vein, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears known as the mastoid arteries.
When someone is afflicted with acute catarrh in the eyes or chest, and this is prolonged and treatment with medicines does not work, then the best treatment in that case is to cut these two arteries.
So if you want to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the artery appears, then tie the patient's neck with a piece of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their positions, the two lower positions behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a sharp scalpel, cutting to the bone, and this should be across the head. And if you wish, insert the scalpel from under the artery and cut upwards, and the length of the cut should be about two fingers breadth.
If the artery is cut, the blood will come out in a pulsating flow, leaping forward repeatedly. If the artery is not palpable, then you should measure three fingers' breadth from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
...sent from the blood six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less, all this depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries.  Then look at the wound; if there remains any peritoneum on the bone, cut it away.
lest a hot swelling occur. Then bind the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Incision of the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases incision of the arteries of the temples or their cauterization, as we have described.
The procedure for their incision is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery visible in the temple should be targeted, for it will be apparent to you from its pulse, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or, in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and obviously. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you put a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on every side; and if the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the hook. Then cut off a portion of it according to how far apart its ends are and contract, and do not cause bleeding; for if it is not severed and cut, the blood will not flow at all. Then evacuate from six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread, and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest putrefaction quickly reach it before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, it will take the place of this same operation or be better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, because cauterization is something that helps to consume humors, and so it is more certain in benefit. After ligating the arteries, it is necessary that the place be packed with worn cotton and firm pledgets be placed on it; and after healing, it should be treated with dry medicines which generate flesh, and with bandages, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your operation, hasten to cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the place with alum and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If you do not have any of that, put your finger there until the blood clots.
And wash the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and tighten it as necessary.  What is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two places with ink.
And lower it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that its ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all.  And this is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scalpels, except that they should be much less sharp than a scalpel, because if they are as sharp as a scalpel, the cold reaches them more quickly and the flesh is not severed quickly. If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will be retained in them.
And the flesh is severed quickly. This procedure is better than any other procedure, and is lighter and easier. Let the distance between the blades be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the scalp from the outside, and nothing from the treatment of medicine has benefited, and you see the patient's face has reddened. And he feels in his forehead a tingling like the tingling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their eyelids having been corroded and their lids clinging together from the intensity of the tears.  Therefore, the ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the sword. It is that you order the patient to have the hair shaved from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision is about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the joining of the jaws.  The distance between each incision should be about three closed fingers. And have with you prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe the blood.
Then you insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it you dissect all the skin between the two incisions together with the membrane on the bone. Then you do that also from the middle incision to the third incision, then you remove this scalpel. And you also insert into the first incision another instrument called a sharp, one-sided knife, smooth and not sharp on the other side, and this is its image:.
And you place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and you push it until it reaches the middle incision, and you cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then you do that in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood has flowed, you squeeze the areas of clotted blood, then you place in each incision a pledget of cotton, and you place over it a compress moistened with syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur, and on the third day you untie the bandage and use much washing with lukewarm water, then you treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and all that you treat wounds with until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone has frequent, sharp, severe, persistent discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and the eyelids ulcerated and the eyelashes falling out. And there is a sharp, painful ache in the depth of the head, and continuous sneezing; then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment in them is this treatment: that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is a sharp, painful ache in the depth of the head, and continuous sneezing; then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment in them is this treatment: that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher transversely, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and let the incision be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples which move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the lips of the incision with worn cotton or a linen thread, then bandage from above with pledgets, and soak the pledgets with syrup and oil, lest a hot inflammation occur. And when you unwrap it and see that the hot inflammation has subsided, then it is necessary to scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins in it.
Then treat it with the drying treatment which causes flesh to grow, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it for the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or something like stone such as iron and glass; or a plant seed like chickpea and kernel and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  When a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, then expose the ear to the sun. Then if you see the stone, instill some violet oil or ceruse into it, then try to remove it by movement of the head or sneezing with a sneeze-inducing agent. And block the nostrils at the time of sneezing, after placing a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment.  If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a gentle curette, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the curette, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear gently.
And seal what is around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a path for the air other than the tube. Then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out by this. If it does not come out by what we described, then take a little of the gum of Nabataea or of the prepared gum with which birds are caught, and put it on the tip of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it into the opening of the ear gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out by all that we described, then proceed to incision before hot swelling or tension occurs.
The method of incision is that you first bleed the patient in the temple and let out blood according to his strength. Then you sit the patient before you, turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe, in its lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel like this.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You do this only if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or with a delicate sponge or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove them. As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear that contains the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by the fire, and let the patient insert one into the opening of his ear, then he should stand on one leg on that side and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with stone after stone until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear and bringing the other end to the fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out, or draw it out with a tube as was mentioned previously for the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small, like a flea or similar; treat it as described in the section, but if...
if its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try to remove it with lint and hooks; this is easier than anything else that gets into the ear.  As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it with what we mentioned in the section on instillations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun; if you see any worms, remove them with lint or fine hooks. If you see nothing, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly; do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I described for the stone.
If they do not come out by the methods mentioned, use the instillations that I have described and which the ancients used to kill worms; you will find this in the section on instillations.  Pouring oils and medicines into the ear should be done with this instrument, and this is its image:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitting copper; if you wish, you can take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the oil, juice, or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the ear and is felt.
by the patient, and let what you pour into the ear be warmed slightly in the fire.  Beware of pouring anything into it that is very cold or very hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of accidental blockage of the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear openings not perforated.
It may also happen that some people experience a blockage in the ear canal from a wound or from newly grown flesh; and this blockage may be deep within the ear opening, invisible to the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible to the eye. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible to the eye is, in most cases, difficult to cure; but that which is visible to the eye should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. And look into it; if you see the blockage, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this form:.
Its tip should be somewhat broad, and limited on one side, while the rest of the scalpel is smooth on both sides, lest it injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage, and the patient feels a heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then insert into the ear a wick the size of the opening, having smeared it with Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are certain the wound will not close up. Or take a wick and moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
And use it. If you see that the ear has swollen with a hot inflammation, then it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another wick smeared with a poultice made with rose oil, until the hot inflammation subsides. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  And use the other treatments which stop bleeding, mentioned in the chapter on styptics.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of warts which appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry. It is necessary to grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If they bleed, cauterize them with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is best after cutting. They often return if some of their roots remain; if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of the cold which affects the eyelids.
Often something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  The treatment is to examine it; if the cold swelling is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, then its treatment is easy, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then carefully peel it from every side until it is released, then hang it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it except after it has passed through the eyelid, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, join it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm from it, for the ointment will heal and join it. If the cold swelling is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the cold swelling with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold swelling with salt water and treat the area with what joins until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the wen which appears in the eyelid.
The wen is a fleshy lump that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid.  This most often affects children, weighing down their eyes and causing them to suffer from colds at night; they are unable to look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore you see them always leaning on their faces, and on them they sleep, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, swollen, and the wen is clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the wen between them. The method of treatment is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick of a size that encompasses the wen on every side, then place it on the wen and press with your fingers from every side to gather the wen in the middle of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of that swelling with a lancet, a horizontal incision, and the incision should not be larger than the incision made in bloodletting. As for the depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the wen.
Then, in most cases, the wen protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat. You pull it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to increase the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and injure it.
If the wen does not appear in the first incision, you should increase the incision slightly gently until the wen appears, then pull it out as we said. Then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
And they place it in the cavity of the incision to dissolve what remains of that swelling. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eyelid surgery.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the normal course, under the normal eyelashes and brows, they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, white spots, and thickening, to the point that it causes blindness.
Eyelid surgery is performed in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by strong medication as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or by excision and suturing, or by the reed, as I shall describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it does not turn, then insert a needle with a thread from beneath the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise inside the eyelid, below the extra hair, with a lancet from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus; then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because people differ in this; some need a portion of the eyelid cut according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less to be cut, all according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the lancet along the two lines which you have marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and let each incision be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then flay it all, then unite the lips with suture using a needle and fine woolen thread, wipe away the blood, and stick what remains of the threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it.  And if you wish, leave the wound without suturing and treat it with things that dry and constrict, for the eyelid will rise when the wound is closed and healed; and suturing is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient; it is a good procedure and there is no danger in it. Another method of blepharoplasty is to mark on the eyelid a shape like that of a laurel leaf, as we have described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, in this manner, in even cuts.
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not straighten for you, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus, and gather the threads together with your fingers evenly, then raise it with a moderate lift.
Raise all the marked skin as we have described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears when you cut or incise, then soothe that inflammation with qirūṭī and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen to the lower eyelid that its edges turn in, so you should use what we have mentioned of cutting and suturing and cauterization with fire and with caustic medication; cauterization with fire or with caustic medication is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and you should perform cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you turn the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you have cut into their ends on both sides where the threads are grasped, then gently bring them together on what remains of the eyelid and tightly pull the two reeds from both sides and leave it for some days, for the stretched skin dies, turns black and decays.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it off with scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it heals, the eyelid will be raised and the edges of the eyelids will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds, make them in this shape and this size in length and width exactly:
Chapter Twelve.
On raising an inverted eyelash from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a fine, smooth silk thread, then bring the two ends together and tie them in a very gentle knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another thin thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient place their head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the superfluous hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upward with the needle and draw into the needle that hair, whether it be one, two, or three, but no more.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards with the thread you attached to it until the needle comes out of the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them; do this until it rises into the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the needle and thread and pull them out, and bind the eye and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a time, then lift it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid.
The stye which is exposed to this is called a hare’s eye, and this stye is either natural or accidental.  The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or incision or cauterization, and so on.
And the way to work in it is to incise that scar tissue and separate its lips, and place a wick of linen between them and tie it until it heals.  And it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was. Rather, use in it things which relax, such as fenugreek and lint with water in which alkanet and flaxseed have been boiled, and diachylon ointment dissolved with something of the fats, and smear the wick with it and treat it with this.
And the principle of its treatment is that you strive by every means that it does not unite in the way it was initially. And this work which we have mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not that the appearance of the place returns to what it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of the stye which forms in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a stye, and it is natural and it is accidental. The accidental one is from a wound or incision or cauterization and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with a doubled thread and you pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at the edges of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
Then if the shape of the eyelid returns to what is proper, otherwise you take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the two incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet and a corner is formed from them. Until when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
And it is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is below what is next to the eyelid, then you gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of emollient and relaxing medicines until it heals.
And if the fistula appeared from an incision or suture or cauterization, it is necessary that you make a simple incision without the hair of the labia as well, as mentioned before, then separate between the lips with a probe as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of fistula, whether it is from above or below, you should work according to what is available to you from the form of the fistula, for it may differ greatly in appearance, and the skillful surgeon devises a way in whatever manner is possible for him.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates to you what is suitable for it in terms of treatment and instrument in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from easy movement and hindering its natural functions.  It is necessary to insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not with the sharpness of cutting scalpels, but rather slightly dull, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, harming the eye with its sharpness.  Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour into the eye salty water to wash it, or instill into it some solution of vitriol dissolved in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen pledget.
And place over the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the astringent eye drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the cutting of the pterygium and the fleshy excrescence of the conjunctiva.
The pterygium is of two types: either nervous, resembling a firm, thin membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a thick, white moisture; if touched by iron, or if one attempts to take it with a hook, it is torn.
And a hook does not take hold of it. Both types begin from the greater canthus until they spread little by little until they cover the pupil and prevent light and the movement of the eye. The method of cutting it is that the patient places his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and insert into it a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and you bend the tip of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium which is next to the pupil, as if you are spreading it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
Lest there result from its cutting a constant flow of tears.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we have said, then the smooth scalpel dissects the end, the image of which is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound and injure the pupil. Then after the cutting, place in the eye some powdered salt or some collyrium of vitriol and leave it until the next day, then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you are unable to insert a needle into it or fix a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medications and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, in this manner: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it loosens and disintegrates and the dissection has taken effect, then immediately instill in the eye some collyrium of vitriol or red collyrium or powdered salt, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation intervenes in the eye; in that case, leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the caruncle…
If the excrescence causes excessive harm to the eye, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it off, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause epiphora. Then instill in the caruncle red or vitriol collyrium until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
Concerning the excision of the pterygium and what grows of excessive flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red fleshy growth may develop, so that it covers the pupil, or comes near to it, or overflows onto the eyelids; and sometimes the eyelids turn outwards, and it resembles a rose-flower.
The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should seize all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that purpose.
Or grasp it with forceps or a small hook; then cut the flesh, piece by piece, until you have removed it all by cutting, and take care of the eye, lest you injure it during the operation; and your cutting should be either with the scalpel which we described in the excision of the pterygium, or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as their picture appears in the chapter following this one.
When you have finished cutting and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with powdered alum, or drop into it some red vitriol or some other such corrosive medicine, and apply externally to the eye a pledget of lint with white of egg, to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overcomes you, and you fear hot inflammation, then leave the eye and treat it with remedies to allay the inflammation, and then return to the operation until it heals; and do likewise with the excess flesh which occurs in the eye of this kind.
But you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by accident, by means of the iron; and do not undertake any of these operations until you see that the organ can bear the operation.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the removal of pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels which grow over the eye, hindering its function and weakening it with the passage of time.  First you should look: if the eye in which the pterygia is present is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then the removal is performed as follows: you should order the patient to place his head in your lap, then you should hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill. The hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:
Or they may be two double hooks in one body, in this manner:
Then, with a delicate pair of scissors, gently pick up those vessels and wipe away the blood from time to time until you see that those vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done midday, facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work, lest you cut anything other than those vessels. When you have finished, instill in the eye green or red vitriol, so that its sharpness may consume what remains of the pterygium. If you cannot remove it all at that time, then bandage the eye with something that will soothe the pain.
And leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is safe, then repeat the operation in the same manner until it is healed. This is the image of the appropriate scissors.
And leave it [and let the patient's head be on the pupil's lap, and another wipes away the tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you] the principle in the operation is to open the eye with fixed or triangular speculums held by a pupil.
Sharp, and let the patient's head be on the pupil's lap, and another wipes away the tears and checks the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice to begin picking from the top of the eye or from the bottom, and it is preferable to cauterize two points on the upper side, one at the end of the ciliary body and the other at its beginning.
And grasp it between the middle finger and the index finger, then hook hooks from below opposite them, then grasp it between the little finger and the ring finger, and lift your hand gently until you see all of the pterygium raised; cut it from the smaller side if you wish with scissors, and if you wish with a delicate scalpel.
And insert the thread onto a needle and scrape away all of the pterygium until it is raised above the conjunctiva, and do not interrupt the cauterization, for the tears and blood will obscure your view.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea; if you can lift it from the cornea by stripping with the thread, it is better, and this may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its place be cauterized for several days, to relieve its pain and prevent hot swelling, then return to work on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the nasal bone to its place.
Physicians call a nasal bone a fistula; if you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine as previously described and it does not heal, then there is no remedy for it except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  And when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
The rasp, which is made of Indian iron, is called the head. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the engraving of a file or a rasp. You place it on the affected part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers, pressing gently with your hand, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this several times, then dress the wound with drying and astringent medications. If the wound closes and flesh grows over it, and the discharge ceases, and it remains for forty days without recurring, festering, or any incident occurring,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the fistula to the nasal passage in this manner:  You uncover the bone again with an instrument or a sharp medicine.  When the bone is uncovered, take a probe of this form:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid and a little further from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
by feeling the air escaping from it when he holds his hand to his nose. Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medications as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the place hardens, then the matter that was flowing outwards will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes completely, and no damage or deficiency occurs in the vision, then it is necessary to purge the patient, then to cup him in the nape of the neck, then to apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without any condition and to gently suck.
Then apply a poultice made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and gum tragacanth to the eye, then bind the eye above the poultice with many pledgets. The method of making the pledgets is to take many soft pieces of lint and fold each piece into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one upon another as much as the eye needs, then bind it firmly with a strong cloth and repeat the cupping with gentle suction, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the binding loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the binding after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise repeat the poultice, pledgets, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cutting the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is ugly because of it, and you want to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is to insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the palate, and pass it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it firmly, then remove the needle and place moistened wool with egg white on the eye and leave it until the threads fall out with the uvula, then treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  And you may pull the uvula from every direction with a circle of cloth until the eye is further protruded firmly, then incise with a thin scalpel reaching the depth of the eye, so that the white moisture flows and the eye descends and withers in place, then pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of condyloma.
This ailment called condyloma is a swelling that gathers in the eye resembling flowing water, but it is not.  The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the swelling descend to the lower part with your eye, then it becomes fixed and does not move, and the light departs and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water. If the descent of the swelling is not achieved by what we mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and incise it a little above the cornea at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the iris until the matter comes out.
Then when it comes out, instill in the eye hot water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for cupping, with a detailed and explained explanation, so take it from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting cross-legged facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and tie it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye with the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of an awl in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while you rotate it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the depth to which the cupping instrument goes is the distance from the pupil to the end of the black part, which is the cornea, for the copper you see in the same viewer with the naked eye because of the clarity of the corneal membrane, then move the cupping instrument upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards time after time, and if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his vision has opened to immediately, with the cupping instrument in his eye, then it will remain still for a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
Then, if it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping instrument gently while you twist it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water some clean Andaranian salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then place on the outside of the entire eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and bind it with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we put on it crushed cumin with egg white. If the cupping instrument does not enter the eye because of its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes, Then you should take the scalpel called the *barīd*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part, and do not exaggerate in the piercing; rather, it is that you make a slight mark for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm bed on which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food that which softens his nature, and he does not move his head to the right or to the left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
And you show him things, then you return the bandage until the seventh day.  And it is not appropriate to do this during treatment or after cupping at your hour, but rather you should avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense vision.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh [day] and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it calms, then at that time you release his sight and place a veil on his face, training his sight from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He exits the house gradually and manages his affairs. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot dispense with observation many times; then he proceeds to the work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a perforated cupping instrument with which he sucks out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country who made that, nor have I read it in any book of the early writers.  And it is possible that that is recent. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for one who is ignorant of them to stand upon:
It is made of copper especially, and its tip is of this thinness itself, triangular-tipped and sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the flesh that grows in the nose.
Various superfluous flesh may grow in the nose; some of it resembles a scorpion with many legs, and some of it is cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some of it is soft flesh not dark in color.
As for that flesh which is soft, neither malignant nor cancerous, then the patient should sit before you facing the sun, and you should open his nostril and cast the snare into that flesh, then pull it outwards, then cut what you have grasped of it with a fine, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If any of it remains that you could not cut, then scrape it off with one of the delicate instruments gently, so that nothing of it remains. If the bleeding overcomes you, or a hot inflammation occurs,
then counter it as is necessary. Or if it was from the malignant tumors, then hasten to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone, then place in the nose after cutting, vinegar and water, or a drink. If the nose opens and the moisture passes from it to the throat,
know that he has healed. If the moisture does not pass as it should, then know that inside there is flesh growing on the top of the porous bones, which the instrument did not reach in the cutting. Then it is necessary that you take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, and make between each knot the space of a finger or less. The patient should be skillful in inserting one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or with whatever he can, after he makes it like a button, and he should draw his breath until it reaches the nasal cavity.
and it comes out at his throat. Often boys in school do this sort of thing, and it is easy for whoever wants to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, the one end that came out at the mouth, and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing that until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then take out the thread, and put in the nose after wiping the blood, a wick that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh. Then, as a final thing, put a lead tube in the nose for some days until it heals. If it needs a drying treatment, use that.
And this is an image of the syringe with which oils and medicines are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small, open lamp, and its conduit is similar.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the nozzle of the syringe flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat oil in it or whatever you wish of juices or liquids.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut off at its first appearance and its entirety excised, then apply to the area either cautery or a burning medicine which serves as a substitute for cautery. If you fail to cut it off until it grows large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not use an instrument on it, for it is a cancerous tumor; and I have often seen those who cut this tumor suffer great harm from it.  But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it is possible to cut it entirely, then let the patient empty his bowels and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the area with what dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of the connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment is rarely effective except in some people.
If any of this happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with blood; bring together the edges of the wound with sutures, then treat it until it heals.  If the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is healthy, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring together the edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered.  Then place on top of the sutures a plaster of palm-tree ointment or another adhesive ointment, and leave it tied for two or three days, then untie it.
And change the dressing and leave it until the threads separate themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as I showed you there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the nodes that appear in the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble a carbuncle, some are smaller and some larger. You should turn back the lip and incise over each nodule and hang it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess flesh in the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hang it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and leave it a while for the discharge or blood to flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatment—for it often does—cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may turn black, yellow, or green, so that decay and ugliness of the teeth reach the gums.
Then you should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and scrape the tooth or teeth on which you see scales and something resembling sand, until nothing remains.  You should do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and so on, until you have cleansed them.
If the substance is removed at the first scraping, well and good. Otherwise, repeat the scraping on another day, and again on a second and third day, until you achieve your goal.  And know that a tooth needs many different shaped scrapers of various forms, according to what is appropriate for your work.
For the scraper used to scrape a tooth from the inside is not the same as that used to scrape it from the outside, nor is the one used to scrape between the teeth.  These are several forms of scrapers that you should have, all prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a tooth for its pain by every means, and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement for it once it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, until there is no alternative but to extract it.
If the patient is determined on its extraction, you should ascertain that the aching tooth is indeed the one to be extracted. For often the patient is deceived by the pain, and thinks that it is in a sound tooth, and extracts it, then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you are certain which tooth is aching, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel, so as to separate the gum from all sides, then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps, little by little at first, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having secured it so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight out, lest you break it. If it does not come out otherwise, take one of those instruments and gently insert it under it from every side, and then move it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a piece of cloth and pack it tightly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull on it with the forceps.  And you should thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
And take care lest you break it, so that some of it remains and causes the patient a greater affliction than his original pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do in their haste and recklessness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often cause great harm to people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or that it is extracted with part of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. Then, after extraction, rinse with a drink or with vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the place – and this often happens – then crush some vitriol and plug the place with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the vitriol does not help you.  The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should have long tips, short handles, and be thick, lest they bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles, so that when you grasp them they do not yield or bend; short in the tips, and made of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with sharpened tips, and at the tip, teeth that interlock to provide a firm and secure grip.
And the tips may be made in the shape of a file, which will also provide a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On extracting the roots of molars and removing broken jaw bones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken, you should apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose tips resemble the beak of the bird called the balaraja.  This is an image of the hooks:
They should be made like a rasp on the inside, or like a scalpel. If you are unable to extract it with these hooks, you should excavate the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument resembling a small lever, which is depicted here:
short at the tip, somewhat thick, and not pointed, lest it break. If the root is extracted in this way, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted here. The first has a triangular tip, somewhat thick:
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted here:
And other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth. We may also use this instrument resembling a large hook, which is depicted here:
Triangular, with a curved tip, somewhat thick, lest it break, and not pointed. Know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, as are all other instruments; they are almost countless, and the skillful and clever artisan may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decays, then examine it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks which were mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a curette, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to control the bone so that it does not escape until the bone is removed. And we dress the area with appropriate medicines. If the bone has a slight decay, then clean it of its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow in a place other than their natural course, it is unsightly, especially if this happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another tooth,
and its extraction or repositioning is impossible, then extract it.  If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron.  The cutting should be done over many days because of the tooth's hardness, so as not to loosen other teeth.  But if it is protruding and you can file it, file it with an Indian file, whose image is this:
It should be entirely of Indian steel, very finely engraved, like the file used to make needles. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, so as not to loosen the tooth and cause it to fall out. Then smooth it and remove it with some forceps.
If part of a tooth is broken and it hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the fracture is removed, it is leveled and smoothed, and it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three
On Binding Loose Teeth with Silver or Gold Threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not worked.
The solution is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever. The thread should be of medium fineness and thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a sound tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then weave back to the side you started from, and pull gently and skillfully so that they do not move at all.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose. Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them together, and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it tied as long as it remains. If it loosens or breaks, tie it with another thread.  In this way, he will enjoy it his whole life. This is an image of the teeth and the manner of binding between two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
It may be that a single tooth or two, after falling out, are returned to their place and bound as we described, and remain. This is done by a skilled dentist.  He may carve a piece of ox bone to resemble a tooth and place it in the space where the tooth has gone. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Concerning cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is from a healed wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are undone.
And beware that the incision is not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then let him rinse his mouth after the cutting with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of lint which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it unite again. If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place; and if the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the removal of a frog generated under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps it may grow large until it fills the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth toward the sun and look at the tumor.
If you see it dark in color or black and hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer. But if it is inclined to whiteness and contains moisture, then pierce it with a probe and incise it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every side.
If blood overwhelms you during your work, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you remove it completely. Then have the patient rinse the mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors; there may appear in the interior of the throat glands resembling those that appear externally, called tonsils. If you treat them with what I mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look; if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with the iron. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with the iron either, for fear of hemorrhage, but leave it until it matures, then either you may lance it, or it may break open of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut. The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided, or lessened somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth.  Have an assistant at hand who will press his tongue downwards with an instrument of this shape:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When the tongue is pressed down with it and the tumor is revealed to you and is in your sight, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil and pull it outwards as much as possible without pulling along any of the membranes.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape: it resembles scissors, except that its two ends are curved, and the edge of each is very sharp.  It is made of Indian iron or Damascene steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he is healed. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut them as I described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which nearly blocked her throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she remained for a day or two. Two branches extended from the tumor until they emerged from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. Then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it. Only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and then to eat.  We repeatedly cut away portions of the tumor over a long time, and the tumor kept replacing what was cut, until this treatment extended for a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery.  It stopped growing, and she traveled away. I did not know what God did with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a tumor of the uvula.
That which is called 'an-`unabah' if it descends as a growth to the uvula and swells, and is white and elongated, is called 'amood'; and if it is thick below and round, it is called 'an-`unabah'. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is ineffective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin, then it should be cut, and whatever was gathered together in a round shape and did not have length, and was bloody or the color of pus, or black, or without sensation, then it should be avoided cutting it.
For there is danger to the patient. Therefore, if you see it in the manner that I mentioned, of its whiteness and length, then seat the patient facing the sun and compress his tongue with the instrument previously described, then pierce the uvula with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils. And it is necessary not to cut from it except that which exceeds the natural state without addition; for if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient shrinks from cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method in cauterizing it without fear or caution. And the way of cauterization in it is only with a sharp medicine, and that is that you place the head of the patient in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take from the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization and mix it with unquenched lime and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it.  And this is its image:.
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat lest something descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you squeeze it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula, then leave it. For it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine around it, then gargle with cold water and treat from the outside with poultices and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take henbane, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and dittany, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil them, the pot being plastered with potter's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid upon which the hollow instrument is fitted. This is made of silver or copper.
And insert the end which has the pome into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is well moistened. Then repeat this several times until it withers. And beware of doing this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  It is only necessary to do this when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit on its end an eggshell, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth, and this is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a fish bone or thorn or something else lodges in the throat.  It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye, after you have pressed down the tongue with an instrument in the sunlight.
So that you may see what is in the throat; and if it does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, then it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged thing will come out with the vomit; or let the patient swallow a piece of beet or the root of a lettuce.
Or let him swallow a mouthful of dry bread; or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, and then let him swallow it.  When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly. Do this several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by what we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than an awl, and at its end a hook. The patient inserts it gently into his throat while raising his head upwards, and he should be careful not to touch his windpipe, lest he cause coughing and push the bone or thorn further in; or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, since he knows the location of the lodged object, and pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medical treatment and it is not effective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have pressed down his tongue with the instrument which I have described to you.
If you see the leech, then pull it out with a small hook or with a soft, firm probe. If you cannot do this, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert into the inside of the tube a heated iron.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from water for the whole day, then he should take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it and rinse his mouth with it, not swallowing a drop, and move the water around from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it feels the water. If it does not come out as we have described, then fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida with the instrument which I described in the fumigation of the tonsils.  Do this several times; then it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is as follows: Take a pot containing hot coals heated by fire, the pot being covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole.  You then fit the end of the instrument into that hole, then you throw in the incense. The patient places his mouth at the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the incense does not escape, until it is known that the incense has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out. If it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation several times. The patient should endure thirst and eat salt and garlic and not drink water; it must come out with this treatment. This is an image of an instrument by which to draw out the leech from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its ends resemble the mouth of a bird; they have the roughness of a file, so if they grip something, they do not let go.
And another instrument, with which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide iron ring, large enough to encompass the mouth above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he cannot remove it at all until it is taken away.
A description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will discuss the cauterization and incision of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not like a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy place is not like a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete, and some should be cauterized while they are raw and not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it putrefies what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the paralysis of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that vital organ, or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor that is not fully mature which is near the rectum, lest the pus fester and penetrate into the rectum, becoming a fistula.
And it becomes something that does not heal.  You should know the time to cauterize tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever goes away, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor becomes pitted, and all other signs [appear].
And it is preferable to perform the cauterization in the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, so that the flow of pus is easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The cauterization should be lengthwise if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
