And the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in general, in all flat areas that do not bend. As for those that bend, the cauterization should follow the shape of that area. But if the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the pus will flow from it for a long time, causing much harm and filth, and perhaps its lips and its core will harden. Some tumors may be cauterized across the body, when necessary, or according to the needs of the affected member.
You should use a single cauterization for small tumors, and for large tumors, a wide cauterization or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  There may be tumors that require the skin to be scraped and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached a point where it has died, as happens in many abscesses and hidden lesions. Some of them are incised with a three-sided incision, and some are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
Some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions.  If it does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, then it should only be cauterized simply. If the tumor is large and has gathered for a long time and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until the next day, then remove some more pus; do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden removal of pus, so the patient may die and you are unaware. Therefore, beware of this matter greatly.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and observe. If the wound of the tumor is small, or the incision is single and simple, use a dressing of linen or worn cotton.  If the tumor is large and the cauterization incisions are numerous,
you should insert a wick into each incision until some reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or scraped away, then you should pack it with worn cotton or linen lint without moisture and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods, treatment, and cauterizations that we have described in many places in this book and in the treatise. If it is winter and the location of the tumor is very sensitive, you should moisten the bandages with a warm syrup and oil.
And place them on the affected area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked in water and oil, or in syrup and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the details, I have described the treatment of each tumor separately, summarized and categorized.
Chapter forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp; they are a type of abscess contained in sacs, which have conditions like a chicken's crop.  Their types are many; some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
Some contain moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on.  Some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the *muddass*, until you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line B to line C. When the moisture has been emptied, remove the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, leaving nothing of it at all; for it often returns if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in the  *marham sirri* if it is present, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it and leave it until the next day; for it eats away what remains of the sac. Then apply the cotton with the *marham sirri* again, a second and third time if needed.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty material, incise it in this manner:
With a firm incision, and place hooks in the lips of the wound and strip it from every side, and exert yourself to remove the peritoneum which contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do what I described to you earlier; and likewise do in the tumor if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I described. The incision on the hardened tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture.  Some of these tumors which are in the head in some people have no moisture at all.  And I incised a tumor in...
The head of an old woman, and I found the tumor like a hard, rough, white stone; it was not possible to break it, and if someone threw it, it would cut him.  And it was not like the other tumors occurring in the head, except for these, like the tumors which appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision, and make their bottoms from their lower parts so that the flow of material to the bottom is easier, then treat them with whatever treatment suits them.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These tumors frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and they may appear in the rest of the body.  That which appears in the neck may be one or many, and some develop from others.
And each swelling of them is within a special membrane, as is the case with the hernias and the tumors of the head as we described, and the types of these swellings are many; some are calcified, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
And what I have seen of them that were in good condition to the touch, and their appearance was near to the color of the skin, moved in every direction, and were not adhering to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor were they deep-seated. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body in this manner, from line ~j~ to line ~b~:.
And peel it from every side and stretch the edges of the skin with a hook or with two hooks or with many hooks if that is necessary, as we said in the tumors of the head, and remove it little by little, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand exceed in cutting or the patient be disturbed, and you cut what you do not need to cut; for if you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from working, then place in the wound powdered alum or some of the styptics which stop the blood.
And bind the wound and leave it until the acuteness of the tumor subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the blood will then stop; then return to your work until you finish, then search with your index finger if there remain any other small polyps, then cut them and cleanse them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the polyp, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a strong double thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm, then stuff the wound with old cotton which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if the polyp is entirely cut, then it is necessary to bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains at all; but if you see that a residue of the bone of the polyp remains,
then it is necessary that you aim at cutting to its root and base, and use the stitching and what we have mentioned; and as for those polyps that contain fluids, apply a simple cautery where the place of their ripening appears to you, and make the cautery from what is below, as we said.
Then, after the cautery, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment and the like, so that it eats away what remains of the corruption; until, when you see that the wound has become clean, treat it with ointments that grow flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with diphtheria, it is necessary that they avoid the incision of the throat, since they do not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak. But those who have a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the windpipe, then it is necessary to use the incision of the throat to escape the danger that occurs from suffocation.
Then, if we want to do that, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are far from it. If the physician is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the membrane which we have described.  He will know that he has incised the windpipe from the phlegm which comes out of it, along with what is torn, and from the interruption of the voice. The wound is left open for a time, and when the time during which suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
And its outline alone, without the cartilage. Then use the medicines which make flesh grow until it heals.  The author of this book said: the explanation of this statement that we have mentioned is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors which they mentioned.
And the patient was close to death, and his life was about to end, they proceeded to incise the throat so that the patient might breathe somewhat at the site of the wound, and be saved from death.  And they were ordered to leave the wound open until the crisis of the illness passes, and its crisis is three days or so.
Then they were ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and threw it at her throat, cutting some of the windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her gasping like someone who has been slaughtered.  So I uncovered the wound.
Then I found that the blood which came from the wound was slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor an artery.  Air was coming from the wound, so I proceeded to sew the wound and treat it until it healed.  And nothing happened to the maidservant except a slight hoarseness in her voice.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this we say that an incision of the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision on the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat’s elephantiasis.
This tumor which is called the throat’s elephantiasis is a large tumor, the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two kinds.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it, but as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which results from the complication of the artery, and there is danger in its incision. Therefore, one should not at all use the iron on them, except what is small of them. If you probe and examine them with a probe and find them to resemble fatty swellings, and not connected to anything of the veins, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with its containing sac if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, then treat the area with what is appropriate of treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and severe pain until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the putrefaction is completed, then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain, and it is contained in a membranous sac, which is its own special covering; and it is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a small blister, larger and smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or it contains moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the probe, as its image will come in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If fluid of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision as I mentioned in other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner as I taught you, and hang it with hooks and peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the sac if you can so that you may remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is perforated during the procedure and you cannot remove it whole—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part remains, however little or much, the swelling will return in most cases.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound at the end with some sharp cauterizing points, and tighten the wound and place on top of it something that soothes hot swelling, and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, then suture its edges and treat it with what unites [the tissues].
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the flow of blood stops, then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter Forty-Seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty, resembling women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C: Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medicine, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast droops and becomes flaccid because of its bone, as happens to women,
it should be incised in its upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each connecting to the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you cannot remove all that needs to be removed because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the type of hard boils contained in a sac, and some contain fluids.  Those that contain fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
from line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the type of a knot of boils, incise it as we mentioned in the incision on boils. If the wound progresses to the point that pus extends, use cautery as we mentioned previously.
Chapter forty-nine.
On the incision of a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called aneurysma.
If an artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong and deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a creaking sound.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round and on the surface of the body. Incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places of the body, and those which are very large; therefore, it is necessary to avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided. As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of the artery, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then peel the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in the ligation of the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the place between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor resolves, and then use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, then you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible together with the skin, then insert a needle below the place that you grasped with your hand, and pass it with a doubled thread until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, according to what I have described to you in tying the grape from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out. Then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then apply a pledget that you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment that involves twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that results from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor appears in an artery and a vein, so too does a tumor appear in a nerve if it receives a blow or is caused by excessive exertion or the like. This occurs most often in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a deep-seated tumor, its color resembling the rest of the body. In most cases, it is not painful, but if pressed firmly, the patient feels a kind of numbness. The tumor is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but not forward or backward. As for that which is in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, for it may cause gangrene. And as for that which is in the head or forehead...
Incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. If it is large, hook it with a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
On cutting warts that appear in the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick; their lips may be inverted, and some are small while others are very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen that resembled mushrooms; they were white, thin at the base, their lips inverted and cracked, and moisture constantly flowed from them. I cut them off; one weighed about eighteen ounces and the other about six.
In cutting them, observe that if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, cut it with a wide scalpel. Have cautery irons ready at hand, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding is excessive, immediately cauterize it. If you see that the patient is cowardly and afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this type, leaving it for two days, then tighten the lead further, continuing to tighten it.
Tighten it repeatedly until it affects the wart so that it is severed and falls off by itself without difficulty. If the wart has a thick base, there is some danger and uncertainty in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of attempting to cut a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and foul in appearance; for it is a cancerous tumor, and we shall discuss cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
On the treatment of an umbilical polyp.
An umbilical polyp arises from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as happens in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from gas that is impacted in it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the protrusion of fat, then the color of the tumor will resemble that of the body, it will be soft without pain, and will appear in various positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position will be, as we have described, more variable.  If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; and sometimes there is a gurgling sound. It increases considerably upon entering a bath and with severe exertion. If it is due to moisture, then it will be soft.
It does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then, along with these signs, the tumor will appear dark. If it is due to newly grown flesh, then the tumor will be firm, hard, and remain at a constant size.
If it is due to gas, its touch will be soft.  The treatment in this case is that you should observe; if the umbilical polyp is due to blood from an artery or vein, or gas, then you should abstain from treating it, for there is danger and deception in that, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned tumors that arise from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical polyp is due to the intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand erect and extended, then you should draw a line with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then you should order him to lie on his back before you. Then you should incise with a broad scalpel around the umbilicus at the place you marked with ink, then you should draw the center of the tumor upwards with a large hook, then you should tie the site of the incision with a strong thread or silk ligature tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then you open the center of the tightened tumor above the ligature and insert your index finger and seek the intestine; if you find that the ligature has seized it, loosen the slip-knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen.  And if you find fat, then draw it out with a hook.
And cut off its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, then ligate it well and return to your work and take two needles, and thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision which you made around the tumor, crossing them, having passed them through. Then tighten the tumor in four places over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the constricted flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it off when it becomes soft and decayed, then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical polyp is from flesh which has grown there.
Or from moisture, then it is necessary that you constrict the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture which you find in it, then treat it with what closes the wound.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medication, and warned against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate, and we have mentioned the cancer which develops in the womb and warned against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that when the cancer is in a place where it can all be excised, like the cancer which is in the breast or in the thigh and similar easily removable members, especially if it is incipient and small, but when it is old and large, then it should not be approached.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that, and the procedure, if it is possible as we said, is to first purge the patient with black hellebore several times, then to bleed him if there is clear fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position which allows for the procedure, then you apply hooks suitable for it to the cancer, then you scrape it from every side together with the skin thoroughly until nothing of its roots remains, and leave the blood to flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, press the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if during the procedure you encounter profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
In the treatment of cancerous sores.
We have already explained in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medication.  That which is treated with cautery is only the ascitic type; the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with cautery.
For that is fatal; if you have treated this type of ascitic cancerous sore with medication and your treatment has not been effective, then look: if the patient is extremely weak, or if he has another illness besides the cancerous sore, such as…
cough or diarrhea or the like, then avoid treating him with cautery, for that is dangerous.  But if you see that the patient is strong, without illness other than the cancerous sore alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is to have the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him to press his belly with his hands and push the water downwards towards the groin, then you take a lancet shaped like this:
pointed at both ends, long, with a defined tip like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestines during the procedure and injure them.  Then look: if the cancerous sore has originated from the area of the intestines…
then you should make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubis. If the cancerous sore has originated from liver disease, then let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; if it has originated from spleen disease, then let the incision be three fingers' breadth to the right.
Also, the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak area. Then pierce the entire skin with the instrument, and insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches an empty space, which is the location of the water, then withdraw it and insert into the opening the instrument which is of this shape:
It is like a tube of thin cane, made of silver or copper or smooth, polished lead. It has a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And its tip may be sharpened in the shape of a pen nib, in this manner:
At its upper end is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately flow down the instrument, and you will evacuate a moderate amount of water at a time, because if you evacuate more than is necessary at one time, the patient may die from the dissolution of his animal spirit.
Or he may suffer a faint approaching death.  But evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you of the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion, then remove the instrument and the water will be retained, because it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which holds the hole in the peritoneum, which I have instructed you to suture in that manner. Then you may reinsert the instrument another day if you see that the patient can tolerate it.
And you will also remove a little water, doing this for several days while you are careful and guarding against error until only a little water remains. If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and patience with thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers, and cauterize the stomach, liver and spleen after removing the water, as I have described above.
Chapter fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages unperforated, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their rectum imperforate; it should be perforated immediately after birth.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, and bind it and keep it there for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it, and afterwards replace it. If you do not use lead, it is possible that the urine flowing to the place will not allow it to close.
As for one whose opening is narrow, it is treated with lead as we have said, for many days until it widens.  Those whose opening is misplaced—some are born with the opening at the end of the rectum and are unable to urinate forward.
So that he raises the urethra upwards by hand, and it is not formed before because the semen is unable to reach the uterus straight, which is a very ugly condition. The method of treatment is for the patient to lie on his back.
Then stretch his rectum with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the head of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, the size of a pen, or as if you were carving something so that its middle protrudes, resembling a penis, and the opening is in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work not to cause bleeding, for this often happens; therefore, stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-six.
On the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a rough fleshy excrescence, and there is benign and malignant.  The benign should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you have completely removed it, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and some of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and some outside, you should remove the ulcer that is inside first, until it heals; then treat the outside.
Because if you treat them both together, you do not prevent the foreskin from perforating.  It may also appear in women, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption; you should excise all that has blackened and threatens to corrupt or has corrupted.
Then apply honey with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and quince, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery of this shape:
For cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean bleeding and the wound if it becomes corrupt. If the slough is consumed and disappears completely at some time, then you should insert a lead tube into the urinary passage so that the patient urinates through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the slough...
This adhesion only occurs in those whose foreskin is healthy and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur due to a wound or swelling. You should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the slough is freed from every side.
If it is difficult to distinguish them thoroughly, you should separate some of the slough and some of the foreskin, since the foreskin is thin and may quickly perforate because of its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the slough with a thin linen cloth moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter fifty-seven.
On cleansing boys and treating what befalls them of ailments.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is especially used in boys, it is necessary that we describe the best method for it.
And the easiest way leading to safety, so I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience, and that is that I found the majority of the surgeons and barbers using purification with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the cautery and ligation with thread and cutting with the nail.  I have tried all of these methods.
I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligation with thread, because purification with the scalpel often causes the skin to retract, since the skin of the foreskin is two layers, and perhaps the upper layer is cut and the lower layer remains.
So one is forced to make another cut and renewed pain, and cauterization does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it may enter into its hole, and as for purification with the nail, the skin may slip and your work will be spoiled, or the boy's skin may be naturally short.
For often they are born thus and do not need purification, and I have seen that. As for purification with scissors and ligation with thread, experience has revealed its excellence to me, because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, because the blade above is like the blade below.
So when you squeeze your hand proportionately with the two blades, you cut with one measurement and in one time, so the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, and no mistake occurs at all. The way to work is first to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little, that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving him until another day, then rejoice him and please him in every way you can, and what he accepts with his mind, then stand him upright between your hands.
And let him not be seated, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon them at all, nor upon anything of the instruments. Then you insert your hand into his urethra, and inflate the skin, and lift it upwards until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then you cleanse it from whatever dirt gathers there, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second below it a little, then grasp with your thumb and index finger the place of the lower ligature firmly and cut between the two ligatures. Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, for that also is excellent.
Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, for that also is excellent. Then apply to the glans from above a pledget of white cloth cooked in rosewater, beaten with fresh, fragrant rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
Let them be sharp-cutting, without curve, with a nail-like point, and let the length of the two blades be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake which occurs in circumcision, perhaps the inner skin is entirely or partially inverted at the cut, so it is necessary to extend it immediately with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you are unable to grasp it with your fingernail, then draw it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and peel it gently.
And cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the glans penis; for if something of the glans penis is cut, it does no harm, so treat it with what closes the wound from the preparations which we described in the chapter on preparations.
And if more of the skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, this also does little harm, so treat it as we have mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its being obstructed by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth, or something similar. If you treat this with the various treatments mentioned in the classification,
and the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient should sit on his knees, then one should mount on his back, and extend his abdomen towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow with what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use its extraction with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver, and is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's quill in thinness, long about a span and a half, with a gentle funnel at its head. The way to draw off the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the catheter, and cut with scissors if any of the wool is in excess, so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then lubricate the catheter with oil or butter or egg white, and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and penis with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then push the catheter inside.
Until the urethra is completely bent, then push the catheter inside until it reaches near the rectum; incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside, then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
This method is used because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then pull the thread with the wool gently; the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it, and the urine will flow; then replace the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
The instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus.  If you wish to instill water or medicine, this is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a hair, hollow throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part where the propellant is, should be only as large as is necessary to block it, so that when you draw something up with it, it will be drawn up, and when you push with it, it will be pushed out to a distance, just as the bellows are used to throw oil in naval battles.
If you wish to introduce fluids into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the fluid and draw upwards with the piston; then the fluid is drawn into the body of the syringe. Then you insert its tip into the urethra, as we described in the catheters, and then push the fluid with the piston.
Then that fluid will reach the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it. And this is the image of a fine clyster also with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature lies. Then take a camel's bladder and put in it the fluid you wish to inject into the bladder, then tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
And warm that fluid slightly over a fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand firmly on the bladder with the fluid until the patient feels that the fluid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin leather and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around.  Then insert a strong thread into those holes as it goes around. Then gather the circle as you gather a bag, after you have put in it whatever you want of fluids, ointments and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the fluid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of stones.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and described the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all of that.  We have also mentioned the stones in which surgery and incision are necessary.
These are the stones generated specifically in the bladder, and those which become lodged in the urethra. I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that stones generated in the bladder most often affect boys.  Among their signs is that the urine leaves the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
Sand appears in it, and he scratches and manipulates his penis; it often hangs down and then becomes swollen, and the anus protrudes in many of them. It is easy to treat in boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and difficult in old men; as for young men, it is intermediate between these.
The larger the stone, the easier the treatment; the smaller, the opposite. If we proceed to treatment, it is first necessary to administer an enema to the patient to remove all the fecal matter in his intestines, for this might prevent the discovery of the stone.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place several times. Then you seat him before you upright, his hands under his thighs, so that the whole bladder is inclined downwards, and then you examine him.
And palpate him externally. If you feel the stone in the space, proceed immediately to incise it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, you should lubricate the index finger of the left hand, if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully grown boy, you insert it into his rectum and search for the stone until, when it is under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then you press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you wish to incise.
And you order a servant to press on the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch the labia upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to stretch the skin under the labia away from the place where the incision is to be made. Then take the scalpel, the shape of which is this:
And incise between the rectum and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left buttock. The incision should be on the stone itself, your finger in the rectum, and you press it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide externally and narrow internally, to the extent that the stone can pass out.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, making their exit difficult because of that.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and rounded, so their exit is easy.  But if one has angles and edges, enlarge the incision a little. If it does not come out like this, then you should try to grasp it with a firm forceps, the tip of which is like a file.
To hold the stone so that it does not escape, or to insert a delicate, curved instrument under it. If you cannot manage it, widen the incision a little. If you are overcome by blood, stanch it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller one afterwards; and do likewise if there are more than two. If it is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision upon it, for this exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either death, or a constant dribbling of urine because the place will not heal at all.  But try to push it out, or attempt to break it with forceps so as to remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, dress the wound with costus, aloes, and myrrh, and bind it up. Place a cloth moistened with oil and wine, or rose oil and cold water, over it to calm the hot inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the place with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm-tree ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If excessive hot inflammation and gangrene, or something similar, should occur in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urination—this is known from the passage of blood with the urine—
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood; for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and decay of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of complication with its appropriate remedy until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind and draw together the thighs to secure the medicines applied to the area.  If the stone is small and has lodged in the urethra, preventing the passage of urine, treat it as I have described before it necessitates incision.
I have often dispensed with incision by means of this treatment, which I have tested. It consists of taking a steel probe of this shape:
The end is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft; then take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, lest it return to the bladder; then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone itself little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through it to the other side; then the urine will flow immediately. Then squeeze what remains of the stone from outside the penis, for it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible due to some impediment, tie one thread below the stone and another above it; then make an incision over the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean the clotted blood from the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other above, so that when the thread is untied after the removal of the stone, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should pull the skin upward so that it returns and covers the wound as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones from women.
Stones rarely form in women. If a woman does develop a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another is that you will rarely find a woman who permits herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or related to you, another reason is that you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially the manual work.  A fourth reason is that in women the location of the incision for a stone is far from the stone itself, requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, though they are rarely found.  If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or procure a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge of this craft.
Then prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order her to do, beginning with examining for the stone.  That is, if the woman is a virgin, she should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If she finds it and presses on it with her finger,
then you instruct her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a widow, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing firmly.
If she finds it, she should trace it from the opening of the bladder downwards as far as she can reach, until she reaches the groin. Then she should incise at the point opposite the middle of the vulva at the groin, whichever side she feels the stone on.
And her finger should not leave the stone, pressing on it. Let the incision be small at first, then insert the probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, she should enlarge the incision as much as she deems necessary for the stone to come out. And know that there are many types of stones.
Some are small and some are large, smooth and rough, long and round, and some have branches. Know their types so that you may know what you are doing. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and leave it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
until the stone comes out. And make sure you have with you the instruments that I mentioned for removing stones from men, to help you in your work. If the bleeding is excessive, and you know by the pulsating blood that an artery has been severed, apply styptics to the area.
And bind it tightly with ligatures, and leave it and do not disturb it, and leave the stone and do not remove it, for the patient may die. Then treat the wound. When the bleeding subsides after some days, and the area becomes infected, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On the incision of the watery bladder.
The watery bladder is merely an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle; and it is called the scrotum.  It may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in one part of the testicle.
So that one might think it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we have mentioned, but this happens rarely. This bladder is generated from a weakness that affects the testicles, so this matter gathers there. It may also result from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture is of many colours; sometimes it is yellowish, sometimes bloody red, sometimes dark like pus, and sometimes watery white, which is most common. The signs by which it is known are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum which we mentioned, the swelling is round, somewhat elongated like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in a special membrane for it, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, a person imagines that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you want to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image we have shown previously; whatever comes out in the wake of the probe, you judge it by what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with the cautery, then you should order the patient to be bled, if he is able and you see his body is full.  Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, and you place many cloths underneath him. Then you sit on his left side.
And you order a servant to sit on his right side, stretching his penis towards one side of the scrotum's skin and towards the region of the groin. Then you take a wide scalpel and incise the scrotum's skin lengthwise in the middle, almost to the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum's skin in half, until you reach the white peritoneum containing it.  Then peel it back and be careful not to tear it; your peeling should be more from the side attached to the testicle.  And complete the peeling as much as you can.
Then you widely incise the peritoneum filled with water and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and extend the peritoneum upwards, but do not touch the containing scrotal skin. And cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure the water will not return. If the testicle emerges from its skin during your work, then when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it, and then join the edges of the scrotum’s skin with sutures.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals. If the testicle is already damaged by another disease, you should ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as mentioned.
If the collected water is in both sides, know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, or if it is easier to do them both at once, then do so.  Then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool that you have soaked in oil or rose oil. And from the outside apply other wool soaked in a medicament and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen.  And place folded cloths on top; these are the pads. And bind them from above with a bandage that has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this way.  Make the straps to tie it with from wet cotton or wool, and gather them tightly in the waistband of the trousers; one end is taken to the buttocks and back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins, and all the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers.  This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
And this is to take a fine, knife-like cautery and with it incise the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as we described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed.  Then take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye, then sear that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and peel off the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can until you excise it all, and be careful not to let the fire touch the testicle.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents more blood loss, and is better than the incision method. If you encounter a hot swelling or bleeding or something else during the incision or cauterization, then you should treat whatever you encounter with the appropriate treatment, as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and does not endure this procedure because of its ugliness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is confirmed by the evidence we have presented.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high chair, then sear the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel. Make the cauterization along the length of the body, and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out. Then place cotton on the area and bind it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization, it may be because the white membrane obstructs the mouth of the wound, preventing the water from coming out.
Then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument for extracting the water of hydroceles, the image of which has been shown previously, or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same way, and the patient should take precautions.
Chapter sixty-three.
On the incision in the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision in this membrane is extremely dangerous and usually leads to death. Therefore, we consider leaving it alone and preserving it to be the best course of action, although I will mention the procedure and the types of tumor.
I say that many tumors occur in the bodies which comprise the genitalia, and this is due to many causes; either from excessive, sharp humors flowing to the genitalia, or from a blow. The color of the tumor will be that of the body, and it will not contain water.
The tumor will be hard, and perhaps it will be hardened, its color dark, and without sensation. It may also be from the twisting and swelling of the arteries, as mentioned previously, or from swelling of the veins.  That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it will not disperse. It should absolutely not be incised. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing of it disperses when pressed with the fingers. This type can possibly be incised, along with other flesh.
The procedure is to incise the skin of the testicle, then to draw the testicle upwards and extract it from the white peritoneum. Free the spermatic cord from the vessels, ligate the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every part of the testicle, if the testicle has adhered to that growing flesh.
Then it is necessary to extract the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritoneums or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular cut.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary to cut it all and extract the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with all other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-four.
On the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted swelling, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the intestines and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this ailment is similar to what has been mentioned before.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary to seat the patient on a high chair, then push the intestine down, then grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels that are close to the penis.
And let a servant hold it for you and stretch it tightly, then you incise with a wide, sharp scalpel an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed, then you dissect from each side as I mentioned to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then you insert a needle with a double thread.
And you tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and you tie it also at its end, then you incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and you remove what has gathered in it of the foul, putrid fluids.
Then you treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal for a period so that the vessels that nourish one of the intestines fall away, there is no harm in that. If the hernia affects all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the intestines with the vessels lest the testicle be deprived of nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen towards the groins from the flanks of the abdomen, and the intestine descends from that hernia into one of the testicles.
And this hernia is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension, and these two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting something heavy, and so on.  Its sign, if it is from an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine. Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that it causes great pain from the beginning suddenly, and the swelling is different, appearing under the skin nearby.
That is because of the exit of the intestine and its passage outside the peritoneum, and the epiploon may exit with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal and epiploic. It may also be with wind, and the excrement may flow into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted. The treatment of this type of illness with iron is dangerous, so one should beware of resorting to it. The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to return the intestine with his hand to the inside of his abdomen if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs, then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise, then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision as much as needed to open the incision with them.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to come out. Then, dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then, insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and with it release the adhesion behind the testicle. Then, with the right hand, retract the skin of the scrotum inwards, and with this, stretch the white peritoneum upwards with the left hand and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
Instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely release the adhesion from behind. Examine with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum; if you find anything, push it down into the abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the skin of the scrotum next to the incision. Then cut the ends of the thread's twists until there are four threads. Then, place some of them on top of each other in a firm manner, and tie the peritoneum—which we said is under the skin of the scrotum—tightly on two sides. Then also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger bone's width of the peritoneum under the skin of the scrotum.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger bone's width of the peritoneum under the skin of the scrotum. And cut all the rest in a circular fashion and remove the testicle with it. Then make an incision below the scrotum's skin from which blood and pus will flow, as we described previously. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound and apply the ligature which we described.
And one may cauterize the white peritoneum which we mentioned after cutting it, cautiously against bleeding, for that often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments of wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a sac, that is, that the vessels should be gently tied below after incising them.
Then incise in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall out, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time elapses, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum; and this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
It may also be treated with the cautery in this manner: the patient lies on his back in your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia horizontally to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum which follows is exposed, then you take a roll and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the end of the roll, and adhere one to the other with the stitching.
Then you withdraw the roll, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
Cauterization is best in this situation because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum's skin.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly. Whoever wishes to treat it should place the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then brings the lips together. And if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, making sure the stitching is secure, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that the physician may know about it if he is asked, and may know how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  As for that which is by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them after you have skinned them, and do not leave any of the membranes on them except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal may desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types: one is that which appears in the area following the perineum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum's skin.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male testicles protruding outward, one of them like a man's penis, and the two like testicles, and the treatment of the three types.
The two types from men and the one type from women, it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type of men from which urine exits from that which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it nor is there any benefit at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
On cutting the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount until it becomes large and its appearance ugly, and it may grow large in some women until it spreads like that of men and becomes involved in sexual intercourse.
It is necessary that you grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it, and do not exaggerate in the cutting, and especially not in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occurs.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may protrude outward like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the tail disease. It should be cut as you cut the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vagina is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be congenital or acquired.  The acquired type is due to a preceding cause, and may be due to an excess of growing flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane.  It may be in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or at its top or bottom. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may prevent menstruation. This is determined by examination by a midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and near, but if it is not apparent, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then proceed to pierce it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread apart. Then she strongly stretches the labia until the thin membrane is pierced and the obstruction is opened. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the site.  The woman should have intercourse every day so that the site does not close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be incised with a broad scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from excess flesh, it should be hooked with hooks and cut.  Have at hand styptics that do not sting, such as acacia, alum, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not quickly close. Keep it in place for several days and use a dry linen wick. Then treat it as other wounds are treated until it heals.  Other fleshy growths may appear in the uterus; these should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If the hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  Their types and signs were mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, have no treatment with iron. Those that are at the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are the ones that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then stretch the warts with a probe or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots. Then, when the blood flows from one of the extremities, apply one of the styptics that stops the blood without cauterization.
Such as acacia,  qiṭṭān, frankincense, and the like. Then raise her legs to the wall for a while, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts and the like. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of the  *ṭarāthīth*, or the juice of *lisān al-ḥaml*, or the juice of the branches of *al-ʿallīq* with a gallnut drink, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of the *ḥāshā*, rough in appearance. It should be cut as described for warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the evacuation of an abscess which appears in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, the hardened tumor, ulcers, hemorrhoids, the consuming ulcer, fistulas, piles, fissures, warts, and the hot tumor.  I have already described all these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this section the hot tumor which appears in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which collect for a period, how its evacuation is done with iron.  You should observe; if the pain of the tumor is initially acute, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever,
and you see it red when touched, do not hasten its evacuation, and treat it with what helps in maturation until these symptoms subside; then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and she gathers her legs towards the lower abdomen, and her thighs are spread apart, and her arms are under her knees, and they are bound with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later.  If you wish to use this instrument, you should measure with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
This harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and the spiral which runs in the instrument should be on the upper side.
And you hold the instrument, and the assistant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened.  If the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel. When all the pus is evacuated,
a soft pledget soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent should be placed in the wound. The pledget should extend outside the incision in the cervix. Clean wool soaked in water in which alchemilla has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment which is suitable for this, until it heals.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which licorice root or long zedoary has been boiled, then return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
It is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated as we mentioned in the section.
Chapter Seventy-five.
In teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural manner.
The midwife should first know the form of a natural birth.  Among its signs are if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to smell the air, and what she suffers from the labor becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is hastened. Know that this birth is in the natural manner, and its emergence is head first with the placenta attached to it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head first, the placenta descends with it and cleanses itself of those impurities well.  Anything other than this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge on its feet, or its hands before its head and feet.
Or one hand or one foot, or its head and hand emerge, or it emerges folded, and perhaps it turns over on its back and similar blameworthy forms.  It is necessary for the midwife to be skillful and gentle with all of these things and to beware of slips and errors.
And I am explaining each form and how to manage it, so that you may learn about it and understand it. If the fetus emerges head first, the natural emergence, and this is difficult for the woman and the labor is hard for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to support her.
And apply a poultice of cooked fenugreek and moist ointments to her womb. Then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and incises the placenta, or incises it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then it is necessary to inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirāj ointment, then after the injection instruct her to sneeze by using hellebore, holding her mouth and nose; then the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary to gently return them little by little.
If they do not readily enter, place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus is dead, then cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately; then he will come out.
