The emergence of the fetus with its feet: If the fetus emerges with its feet, then it is necessary to push its feet upwards, then turn the fetus little by little until you straighten it, then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to sneeze using hellebore; then it will emerge. If it does not emerge as we have described, then return the fetus little by little until you return it to its natural position; then it will emerge easily. If it is prevented by all that we have described,
Take mallow saliva, fenugreek saliva, shirāj ointment, and dissolved gum, and grind them all well in a mortar, then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharisif.
If you see that her lower parts have softened, make her a pessary of myrrh and she will bear it. If the pessary remains for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, blocking her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze the lower part of her abdomen; then the fetus will emerge immediately.  The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands:
If it emerges in this manner, gradually insert your hands, then let the woman lie on her back, and lower her hips while she is lying on a bed, and she should clasp her hands, then gently squeeze above her abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower parts are raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both of her legs and move them vigorously, then gently squeeze above the sharisif until the fetus ascends, then the midwife inserts her hand and gently adjusts the fetus.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges.  The emergence of the fetus sideways, with one hand hanging down:  gently try to return its hand; if you cannot return it, make the woman stand and walk. If she cannot walk, let her lie down...
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously, then use the poultice I described from the ingredients. If labor pains seize her, seat her on a chair, then treat the matter of returning the hand and straightening the fetus to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back: the midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then gently turn it and straighten it. Once it is straight, apply to its head what we described of the ointment and poultices.
Then tell her to move a little by little. If she does that, seat her on the chair and tell her to lean to her right side, then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the feather; then it will emerge. The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture. If it is attached to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it suffocate and die; then push the fetus inward until it is in the proper position.
And instruct the woman to strain repeatedly, then it will emerge easily.  The emergence of twins or many fetuses: Know that twins are frequently born, and three or four may be born and live, though this is rare; as for five, it is something outside of nature, and they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by a single placenta, and they are separated by a membranous partition between them, attached to the navel of each one. And they may emerge as a single fetus emerges, naturally and easily, or they may emerge in a difficult manner as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and become as we have mentioned among the unnatural forms, then treat them as we described. Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and let her contrive for every form with whatever means presents itself to her that leads to safety.
Mentioning what is conceived in the womb of fetuses which are aborted: One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed for me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing, All-Mighty.  All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may occur to her one day, and she will meet it with what is appropriate.
Chapter seventy-six.
On removing the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I have mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and the matter compels you to work with your hand, then you should look: if the woman is healthy and strong and does not have any of the symptoms which one fears might cause her harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be inclined downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should support her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is extracted.
Then her womb's mouth should be moistened with moist ointments, along with mallow and fenugreek sap and flaxseed, then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saps, then gently insert it into the womb, then seek with it a place to hook the fetus with hooks, and the suitable places for that.
Look, if the fetus is positioned to come down head first, then hook into its eyes, or its nape, or its mouth, or its palate, or under its chin, or its clavicle, or in the places near the ribs, or under the cartilages.
But if it descends feet first, then in the pubis and the middle ribs.  It is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand and place its curve between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and hook into some of those places which we have mentioned.
Until you reach something firm, then hook another or a third opposite it, if needed, so that the traction is even and does not incline to one side, then pull with a steady pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but moved in every direction.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  It is necessary during this to relax the pull; if it is obstructed from one side, it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is obstructed. If some of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
She should raise it slightly, doing this until the whole fetus comes out. If a hand comes out before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until, when it is completely out, you cut it.
Either at the joint of the wrist or at the elbow.  And thus it is necessary to do with the other hand and with the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences difficulty in exiting, or if there is accumulated water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water comes out, or puncture it with the instrument called the perforator, the image of which comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, then it is necessary to split the skull or perforate it with the perforator as we said, then remove the bones with the hooks. If the head comes out and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be split until the moisture in the chest flows out.
For the chest then contracts. If the chest does not contract and it is not possible for it to come out, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a tumor, then it is necessary to puncture it until the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is feet first, then pulling it is easy and straightening it towards the mouth of the womb is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and split the abdomen or chest until what is in them flows out.
If all the limbs are extracted and the head returns and is retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, then insert the hand into the neck of the womb and search for the head with it and pull it with the fingers to the mouth of the womb, then insert one or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has contracted due to a hot tumor affecting it, then it is not necessary to be forceful towards it, but it is necessary then to use pouring of fatty moist things, and use poultices, and sit in waters that relax and moisten.
And as for if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then use what we mentioned for the live fetus. If that is not possible, then cut pieces and extract it.  It is necessary not to leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot tumors that appear in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, then descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have indeed seen a woman who had conceived and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb and swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I applied some strong drawing ointments to it.
Then a bone came out of the place, then after some days another bone came out. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is a place where there are no bones. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted from it many bones from the bones of the head of the fetus.
I continued doing this until I extracted many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived like that for a time, with a little pus coming from the place.  And I have only come here to relate this rarity.
Because in it there is knowledge and aid for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are the images of the instruments which are needed in extracting the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is a binding instrument with which books are bound, having two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binding instrument and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width about a finger and their length a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same kind of wood which have been fastened in them, their length being half a span or a little more, and their width about two fingers or a little more, and these two guides.
are those which enter into the mouth of the womb so that it may be opened by them when you turn the screws; an image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guides at its end as you see, the length of each guide being about a span and their width two fingers; so if you wish to open the womb with it.
Then seat the woman on a bed with her legs dangling apart between her thighs, then insert these two guides gently into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, to the extent that you want to open the womb. Until the midwife does what she wants. An image of another screw mentioned by the ancients.
An image of the instrument which pushes the foetus.
An image of the forceps with which the head of the foetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, with which it cuts and crushes.
An image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when the fetus is pulled with it.  Image of a hook with two barbs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All of these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his skill among people. Therefore, do not disdain any instrument, however prepared, for there will be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then sneeze into a handkerchief, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as rue, pennyroyal, dill, chamomile, wormwood, marshmallow, and centaury; all of these medicines, or some of them. Submerge them in water, and place them over the fire; then place a silver tube over the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb; then have her sneeze as we said.  The placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in  surgine oil or mallow sap.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we have described, until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little along the vulva.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then increase the amount of pulling; then it will yield and free itself from the adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described treatment with sneezing and treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid violence in pulling it again. But it should be tied—what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb—to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the quadruple ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out. However, if it becomes putrid, a bad odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient. Therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the coals.  She holds it until that incense is gone and replaces it with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their rectums imperforate; a thin membrane closes it.  The midwife should perforate this membrane with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she applies wool soaked in syrup and oil, and then treats it with ointments until it heals. If she fears it will close up, she places a lead tube in the opening for many days, removing it whenever the child wants to defecate.
The rectum may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor; this adhesion should be incised, and then treated as described above, with the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the rectum externally or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus flows from it. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the perineum and to the intestine, and some may not penetrate.  Those that penetrate may be recognized by the fact that feces and flatus pass through them when the patient defecates.
And worms may come out of them. There may be fistulas, if they are in the space, penetrating to the bladder and to the urethra.  And some may penetrate to the hip joint and to the sacrum.  A fistula penetrating to the rectum is also known differently from one that does not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, flatus, or worms, as we said, emerges from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating, and proceed to treatment.
If the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urethra, the indication is the passage of urine from it and the failure of the site to heal with medications.  As for when it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and pain is present in that area, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment—all these penetrating fistulas—there is absolutely no cure for them.
And their treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt to treat them. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I will describe and as I have tested.  It is that you place the patient on his back between your hands.
And raise his legs upward and incline his thighs towards his abdomen. Then insert the copper or lead probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, uncovered, without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating; do not exert yourself on it, for there is no cure for it, as we have said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments from which benefit is hoped for is that you heat a thin cautery iron according to the width of the fistula, as has been described, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches near the anus, then return it two or three times.
Until you know that all of that excess, congealed flesh resembling a bird's quill has been burned. Then treat it with a pledget soaked in butter until the burned flesh is removed, then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment.  But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and there was a thick partition of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the fistula on the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass along the incision with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the tip of the probe ends, and the probe is freed and falls out. Then you cleanse that congealed flesh resembling a bird’s quill, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood overcomes you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, then the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a strong medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with pledgets soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the area suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are medicines which have astringent and drying properties; for when it is healed, it is cured, and you do not fear a recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a partition of peritoneum or flesh, and it is near to penetration, then the treatment is only by way of hope and expectation; that is, to employ one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can easily insert pledgets and treat it from near; and do not overdo the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient involuntary passage of stool. Then treat it as we mentioned, for perhaps it will heal, as we said.
Or to penetrate that partition and pierce it after incising it near the anus, as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all that hardened flesh which is in the incision as far as you can, thoroughly.
Then suture the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, and the fistula remains open inside the anus; this will be easier for the patient.  And the fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is at the side of the anus, towards the surface of the body with the skin and the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end like a shoemaker's needle, in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom, if it is penetrating into the margin of the anus from inside, nearby.
Then bring the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread, and bring both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until that flesh between the ends of the thread is cut and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that on account of the muscle lest you cut it. Then treat it as we have described until it heals.
The image of the hooked bistoury with which fistulas are incised: its curvature is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the opening of haemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Haemorrhoids are of two kinds: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, in most cases, less moist; a yellow liquid or a little blood flows from them constantly, and they are the colour of the body.
The treatment of those which are inside the rectum is that you should order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum is protruded and the haemorrhoids appear to you.  Then quickly seize them with hooks or grasp them with your fingernail and cut them off at their base; if the hooks do not hold because of their moisture and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of goatskin, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them, and then sprinkle on them after cutting some sharp powders to act in place of cautery, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the other treatments until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and make it come out quickly when the patient strains. As for the haemorrhoids outside the rectum, their treatment is easy.
That is, you take it with your fingernail or hook it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use a ligature in this way: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the haemorrhoid upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and wind the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the haemorrhoid tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the haemorrhoids and leave one unligated.
so that the blood flows from them, then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or kyrios oil and place it on the rectum and tell the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall off. And when the haemorrhoids have fallen off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
And as for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from dryness of the faeces and retention of the humours. If they become chronic and medicine does not work, then it should be scraped with a scalpel blade or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust which prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than before until it bleeds well and is excoriated, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts and ants.
A corn is a round nodule, the colour of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs on the whole body, but especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the corn and grasp it with a hook or a snare and pull it out from its root, then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it as described previously in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, clustered, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off; and if you wish, cauterize it with fire or a caustic medicine, as previously described.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant, it is also a small, clustered, thick protrusion on the surface of the body, extending deeply; and if it is exposed to cold, one feels a sensation similar to the sting of ants.
It occurs throughout the body, but most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made from a strong goose or eagle feather, and place this tube over the ant so that the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides; then rotate the tube with your hand until you incise around the ant.
Then push it into the depth of the flesh, and the ant will be pulled out from its root. And if you wish, you may do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and wired to make it easier for the fingers to grasp and twist it.  And if you wish, cauterize the ant as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the shapes of the instruments used for injections in the treatment of rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
Syringes may be made of silver, china, or cast or hammered copper.  These instruments may be made in small and large sizes according to the users. Those used to treat small children should be small, and those whose rectums are narrow or painful should have very gentle syringes. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is attached, is wide in this manner, having a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see.  Its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is solid and very smooth, except that on one side there are two holes and on the other side one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a gimlet or slightly larger. The bladder, in which the medicines are placed, is made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, fashioned in the shape of a small pouch, its diameter being one span.
Many holes are pierced in a circular fashion, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then a strong thread of doubled yarn, of ten strands or so, is inserted into these holes and the bladder is gathered together like a pouch. When the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly bound at the head of the syringe between the stones, then injected. This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the holes:
The method of injection is to fill the pouch or bladder with warmed medicine, to the extent the patient can tolerate. Beware of it being too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A cloth or blanket is placed beneath him. Then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or the like, and gently inserted into the rectum. Then the attendant takes the pouch in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him restrain himself as much as possible. If he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial. If it is injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hastened, repeat the injection until he recovers.
If there are painful hemorrhoids or tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very fine and smooth, smoother than those used for injecting children. This is an image of a fine syringe with its skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I will now mention here its treatment by hand and by medications completely.  I say that wounds differ according to the thing that causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as a blow from a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to the locations of the body, such as a wound that occurs on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and so on from the organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple wounds of the head especially, because the compound ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and only the skin has been split, and the wound is large and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and incise it, using a cupping instrument, in that place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little; and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
And warn the patient against fullness, and thick drinks and foods; and if something prevents him from bloodletting, then let him reduce food and not approach meat or drinks.  And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone, or with a drink that contains astringency.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the powder which I describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. And if the wound is large, and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then join it by stitching, as I describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs down, and its attachment is only by a small ligament, then cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that produce firm flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to nourish himself with foods that contain firmness, such as heads and brains and the like.
If gangrene occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the gangrenous part and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding blood, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, then sever it with the scalpel and tie it. And if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, then their treatment is easy; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
before the air changes them.  The preparation of the powder is that one part of frankincense, two parts of acacia, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken; all are ground, sifted, and the wound is packed with it and firmly bandaged until it adheres well.
It coagulates with the blood, then bandage it from above with bandages and leave it; do not untie it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not separate from it until it heals.  The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and acacia.
This action may also be done with cobwebs in many large wounds if it is skillfully applied and bandaged, and the wounds are fresh with blood, before the air changes them; but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer,
then applying this powder is meaningless; treat it as mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until pus forms, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound and it is slight, pull it with a splint. I have mentioned the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck; if the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that strongly constrict, like verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of the organs, and what you treat it with should be from soft medicines, such as ceruse washed many times with fresh water, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner and moist ointments, but if the wound is large, use suturing or bind its edges with pledgets, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and a cavity forms in its bottom where pus collects, lance it in its lowest place; if an artery has been severed in the wound and you perceive its blood flowing, ligate it or tie it off or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx and the cervical vertebrae are unharmed, bring together the edges of the skin by suturing over the windpipe, and do not touch the windpipe, but smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tightly bind it and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters in it, extract all of them or as many as you can, and leave what you cannot remove until the wound suppurates and heals, and it will be easier to remove them.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone becomes suppurative in the wound and forms a fistula, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, on the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see a depth to it, observe it; if air escapes from it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it has no depth and is fresh with its blood, do not initially apply the powder to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depths.
It will then recede to the heart and kill the patient. But put in it an attractive ointment, and dress it twice a day. If you do not have an ointment, put a used piece of cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it from the moisture, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
So that what collects in it may flow out. And if three days or more have passed since the injury, and the patient has not experienced convulsions, a bad pulse, or shortness of breath, and you see that all his conditions are good, then know that the wound is healthy; treat it with cautery and other treatment until it heals.
If its sight is difficult and the pus flows continuously, know that it has become a fistula; treat it in its chapter.  The patient should lie on the wound so that what collects in it may flow out. If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the previously described suturing.
If it is large, cauterize it; if small, use powder. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments from it, explore the wound and quickly remove those fragments if they are free; if they are not free, leave them until...
the wound becomes septic, for then it is easy to remove them. As for the other wounds occurring in the other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which affects the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large wound may allow the exit of one or several intestines, and their replacement and suturing will be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way; its replacement may be more difficult, and if the intestine is not immediately returned to its place it swells and thickens, making its return difficult.  Therefore, the medium-sized wound is preferable, because returning the intestine is not as difficult with it as with the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types.  Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body; the other two sutures are specific, applicable to all wounds, but they are most especially suited to the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine has emerged and its replacement is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which anise, rue, and spikenard have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the inflation resolves.
A medicament with astringent properties may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.  If you see that the inflation has resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which alkanet or madder has been boiled, for it will facilitate its return with the greatest ease.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound with the instrument with which fistulas are incised. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to after necessity and the failure of other methods. This is the image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel, but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small forceps, as you see. If the wound has widened and the intestine has entered, then it should be replaced in its natural form.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is large and is in the lower abdomen, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; and if it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always make the side with the injury higher than the other side. This should be done in large and medium-sized wounds.
As for small wounds, position it as best you can. Then have a skilled assistant at hand who holds the entire wound with both hands and brings its edges together, then reveals it bit by bit to the person doing the suturing.
This is the description of a single common suture: take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert a needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  You will have brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then tighten with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then connect it with thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until you complete the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin; therefore, they should be of medium thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals.  Nor should the stitches be too far apart, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles so they do not injure the patient while sleeping, and make for him pledgets of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because it may be sufficient to suture them with one or two needles, or thereabouts.
As for the method of suturing for the general wound, it is to bring together the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle threaded with moderately fine twisted thread. Then, when the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle to the side from which you began.
So the thread will be interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of a bag which is tightened with it.  And make the space between each stitch the width of the little finger. This stitch is known to everyone.
With this stitch, I sewed a wound that appeared in the abdomen of a man who had been wounded by a knife. The wound was more than an inch wide and about two inches of the small intestine had protruded; the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned it after its intestine had protruded from the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he was cured. He lived many years afterwards, managing all his affairs, although the physicians had judged that he would never recover.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines.  I applied worn cotton to the wound twice a day and dressed it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's words: begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. When the needle has passed through the skin and into the muscle going straight along the whole length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle, and I passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. When I had passed it through, I passed it again through this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on that side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it into the edge of the peritoneum on its side until you pass it through entirely.
Then begin again from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out through the skin nearby. Then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out through the skin on its side. Do this again and again, one after another, until you have sewn up the entire wound in this way. In short, the description of this stitch is that you sew it like furriers sew fur.
That is, you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and you leave it once from the other side until you are finished. As for the second type of special stitch which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact wording:
It is that you sew it in the way that some physicians sew it, joining each part to its naturally corresponding counterpart, so that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the gut to its other edge, which is as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the gut closest to you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside through both edges of the peritoneum. Then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the gut which is on the opposite side. This type of stitch is better than the easy general stitch, which is the stitch mentioned earlier. This is Galen's exact wording. And some have said that the stitching of the abdomen may be straightened with two needles. That is, you insert one thread into both, and begin by inserting the needle from your side, passing it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, passing it to your side, according to the stitching of the saddlebags.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its treatment is the same as the treatment of other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestine and stitch it and secure it, then apply to it the soldering irons.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out and the peritoneum and gut are joined together. Then treat it as you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, apply to it from when it begins to suppurate, worn cotton, and change it twice a day as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound might involve the principal organs in pain,
Then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines—and this often occurs—then inject him with a lukewarm black astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what was thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what was thin of them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound occurring in it at all, because of the abundance of its vessels and their size, and because of the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what has protruded from the wound is the omentum, and you find it soft, then replace it according to your replacement of the intestine, the same.
If a period of time has passed and it has become green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries, then cut below that ligature.
And make the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to remove it and extract it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.  Mention of the wound which occurs in the intestine:  But if a perforation occurs in the intestine and it is small...
it is possible that it may heal in some people, for I have seen a man who was wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged, so I treated it, though I did not expect it to heal. So I continued to treat him until it healed and the place united. When I saw the place had united, I feared for the patient that a bad accident might happen to him in his abdomen, but no bad accident happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of conditions. He eats and drinks and copulates and enters the bathhouse and exercises in his service in good health.  Some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sewn in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the lips of the wound are brought together, then one of them, with its mouth open, is placed on the lips of the wound.  When it grasps and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, for it will adhere and not come undone.  Then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and sew the wound, for those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm at all happens to the patient. And it is possible that the intestine may also be sewn with fine thread.
Which is drawn from the intestine of an animal adhering to it after being inserted into a needle; and that is that the end of this thread is taken from the intestine, then it is carefully drawn out, then a fine twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is inserted into the needle. And in it is the intestinal thread, and with it the intestine is sewn, then it is returned to the cavity. And this type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
In the treatment of the cold and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever organ it may be, a fistula, and we call it a cold, and a fistula.
It is in reality a hardened, matted, white mass with no pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, always discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture is interrupted.  This moisture may be abundant or scant, thick or thin.
And I have already stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is so because of one of nine reasons; one of them is either because of a deficiency of blood in the body, or because of its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a general epidemic in the area; or because of a particular characteristic of the area.
Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a general epidemic in the area; or because of a particular characteristic of the area. As happened in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one or more bones in it. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
It is necessary here that I inform you of the treatment of fistulas and sinuses which is done by hand. Know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; some of them end in large veins and arteries or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the compound joints such as the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ and so on.
Then those fistulas of this kind are among the difficult-to-cure diseases which do not accept treatment and working with them is troublesome and ignorant; but if there are fistulas which have not reached these places which I mentioned and I hoped and trusted in their treatment.
And you wished to reach the knowledge of that, then take a probe of copper or iron if the fistula runs straight, and explore it with it. If there is a bend in the fistula, explore it with a probe of thin lead because lead softens its body.
It will become smooth upon entry and bend towards the bend. If the fistula has many openings you cannot determine them with the probe, then inject one of its openings; for the moisture which you inject into it will pass towards the other openings and flow.
Then thoroughly investigate in whatever way you can to know if there is a bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings.  Determine all of that to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and the presence of pain when you touch the area.
And similar signs and causes of the swelling. If you have determined all of that with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence; and that is that you should look, if it were...
The fistula is outwardly near or in a safe place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I have previously described and remove what is in it of the  matted material, the putrid flesh, and the excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it deeply as much as you can, then cleanse it of all its putrid flesh.
Then use a pledget soaked in strong medications and insert it into the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until that strong medication eats away all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then treat it with ointments that cause healthy flesh to grow until it heals. If it does not heal with that, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and that is certain to you, then examine it and incise it as I have told you, if nothing prevents you from [doing so].
a nerve, or a principal organ, as we said. If the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was doing,
know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption.  Expose everything on it again, and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort, then dress it. If it heals, otherwise, know that that corruption is in a deep recess; expose everything on it again.
and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is a small bone and you can extract it, then extract it with fine forceps suitable for that.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any of them behind to the best of your ability. If something of the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh if it is there, and extract the bones.
If you are not able to extract the bones at once, apply to the wound something that will cause it to suppurate, and leave it for some days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones suppurate, and keep the wound from closing the incision which you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments; for when they have suppurated those ligaments and loosened the bones, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is one large bone, such as a bone of the leg or a bone of the thigh or the like, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappears, then dress the wound.  If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends; then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you of a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to make it an example and an aid to your treatment.  This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause originating within his body, until the matter reached the leg and swelled with a great swelling, and there was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the doctors until the swelling opened.
And much matter flowed from it, and he was badly treated until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and the humors of the body. A number of physicians treated him for about two years, and among them there was no one skilled in the craft of surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in a great stream, and the man’s body was thin and his color yellow. Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
Then I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg.  I began by incising one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone. I found it corrupted, eroded, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow.  I excised what was revealed to me.
And I removed the corrupted bone, and I think that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I cut and excised, and that I had eradicated it. Then I proceeded to dress the wound with cauterizing medicines for a longer period, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
Then I found the corruption continuous with the bone. I also excised what appeared to me of that corruption. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite.  Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I had cut about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and completely. This repetition in its operation and incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient, his poor tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he frequently suffered from excessive vomiting and fainting.  Then he recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs and was not afflicted in walking by any harm whatsoever. If a projecting bone in a part of the body is fractured,
it should be excised in this manner: you take a ligature and tie it around the end of the projecting bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it to the flesh under the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you expose the flesh from the place that you wish to excise so that the saw does not injure the flesh, and you place a firm piece of wood or a board under the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone. The excision should be slightly above the site of the corruption, so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone that is not apparent to the senses, requiring you to excise it again.
If the bone is corrupted and is not projecting but its parts are connected, and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the wood from below, and then excise it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished excising it, excise it from the other side, and let the excision be a little distance from the corruption, as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and excise the bone where it joins from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy except amputation. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or the metacarpus of the foot, its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the corruption however it appears to you, and amputate it and clean it in whatever way you can.
And in whatever way it is straightened for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their setting, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their hardness and looseness. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
And know that the works themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this profession, for it is from the skill of the profession and witnessing various types of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I am depicting for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
making them examples to be imitated and a measure by which you measure others, God willing.  Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In cutting off limbs and sawing bones.
Limbs may become putrefied, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat that corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing stopping it,
it is necessary to cut off that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may thereby escape death or a great calamity which is greater than the loss of the member.  And a sign of this happening to someone is that the member blackens until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after the blackening, until that putrefaction spreads to what is next to that member and affects the whole body; then hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some vermin, such as a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or the sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has affected the hand, cut it off from the origin of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it affects the bones of the arm.
Likewise, if it affects the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it reaching towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient, and use other treatment as much as possible.
And likewise you do with the leg: if the corruption affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it affects the metatarsus, cut off the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and leave the patient to death. The method of cutting off or dissecting a limb is to tie a ligature below the place you want to cut and tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or dissected; and you should place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy part, which would cause the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. And I tell you of an example that occurred to a man in his leg, this very condition that I describe to you; that is, that blackness with burning like fire occurred in his leg.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it took the whole leg. So the man, of his own accord, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb, with the intensity of the pain and burning he felt, cut it off at the joint, and he recovered.
Then, after a long time, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand.  So he came to me, and I attempted to restrain that corruption with the medicines I applied to his hand, after cleansing his body; but the corruption did not stop, and it continued to spread into the other finger.
Until the corruption seized the hand.  He urged me to cut off his hand, but I refused, hoping to restrain the corruption, and also fearing for his life if I cut off his hand, for the man's strength was failing. So, when he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then it was reported to me that he cut off his hand entirely and recovered.  I have recounted this story to be of help in cases of this kind of disease, and to be a guide to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter eighty-eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy members and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers matter, then bursts or is incised, and all the matter within it comes out, and the place remains empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not reached the bone, nerve or ligament; therefore it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects one of these members.
Then it is called a sinus or a fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin as it extends, especially if it is thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection.  This is to look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and becomes the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing; do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you; this is to take the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood, and pour water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been cleansed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and wine mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and wine closes the fistula, especially if the wine has a binding and drying property.  When you have done this several times and are certain that the corruption of the fistula has gone, then inject it with something that will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine, or inject it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the fistula is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to enter,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is also wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter and no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest place so that the pus flows downwards, for if the pus is retained in the depth of the fistula it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the member so that the pus flows out easily, as best you can, so that nothing at all of the pus is retained in it.
As for the cauterizing medicines which are applied externally to the fistula, and how they should be applied, take some of the appropriate cauterizing ointments, spread it on a linen cloth, the size of which should be sufficient to cover the whole fistula. Then, with scissors, cut an opening opposite the mouth of the fistula, slightly larger than the fistula’s opening. Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to fit the opening, and place it there, keeping it away from the mouth of the fistula to facilitate the exit of pus, and do not remove the larger cloth until later.
Several days; as for that on the mouth of the fistula, remove it at all times so that whatever pus has collected in it may come out, and so that you may know whether what flows from it is much or little, mature or immature.
Also, examine the fistula itself: does the patient feel pain in it or not, and is there swelling or not? If your treatment continues in this way, and you see that the material that comes out is slight at the opening of the fistula.
Know that the fistula has adhered or is close to adhering; then resolve it the next day or on the third day. When you have opened the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment. If after a long time thin pus comes out of the fistula, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If after many days immature pus comes out of the fistula, know that the fistula has not healed. If the healing of the fistula is slow,
increase the drying medications, and let the medications be moist in consistency and dry in strength, like palm ointment if an additional amount of litharge has been added to it. Among the most effective treatments is to take palm ointment.
and mix it with rose oil, and sprinkle on it old wine of moderate consistency, then knead it and use it.  He may do something similar if palm ointment is not available: honey, cooked until it thickens and is used; or take myrrh, aloes and costus, crush them all and sprinkle them on honey while it is on the fire. Then apply it on a cloth and bind it to the fistula; or apply honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the medicaments, and apply the cloth and bind it.
Long zedoary, the root of the asphodel,  the powder of  cistus and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or mixed. Crush and sift them and sprinkle them on the honey described above and use it. If the fistula persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
If it has affected the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it like a fistula, as described above.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the injured nail, the excision of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excess of flesh which grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and sometimes grows in the other fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes putrid and foul, until it eats away the root of the nail, and perhaps destroys it entirely; and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, so that it has a foul smell, and the tip of the finger becomes broad and its colour livid.  If you treat it with what we have mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective,
then you should cut away with an instrument all the superfluous flesh which grows from the nail, and then cauterize the wound; for cauterization is very useful in this case.  But if the bone is sound and the nail also sound,
and the angle containing the nail has compressed the flesh inwards, causing it to be pinched and injured, then you should place a thin pledget under the angle of the nail which pinches the flesh, and raise it upwards, and cut away that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh some of the cauterizing, corrosive medicines until it is all gone; then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the corruption has affected the bone, then you should cut away that bone and remove it, for it will not heal at all so long as there is corrupt bone in it.
If you see that the ulceration and corruption are spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints, as mentioned before, and then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury, and severe pain results, you should first bleed the patient.
Then incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision the flesh under the nail; for by so doing you would cause the patient severe pain and it would be a cause for the growth of superfluous flesh in the place. Then treat the place with what relieves pain.
As for the superfluous finger which grows in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in it, or it may have a nail; and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the joints of the finger. Those which grow in some of the joints of the finger do not move, but those which grow at the joint of the finger may move.  If it is only fleshy, its excision is easy; and that is to cut it off at its root with a broad scalpel.
As for those whose growth is at the root of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting them. As for those which grow on the finger at one of the joints, you should first cut away its flesh in a circular cut down to the bone, then saw through the bone with one of those saws suitable for this.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs between the fingers, this often happens, and it is from what a person is born with, or it occurs from the healing of a wound or a burn with fire, and so on.  It is necessary to incise this adhesion.
until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them a pledget or a piece of lint soaked in rose oil, lest it quickly adhere; and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals properly.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, incise this adhesion according to what is appropriate and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the cutting of haemorrhoids.
Haemorrhoids are twisted, thick, swollen veins filled with black, stagnant blood. They occur in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens, such as porters, and those who work with heavy loads.  First, the body should be purged with black hellebore, several strong purgations.
Then, bleed the patient from the basilica vein. As for treating them with iron instruments, there are two methods: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to excise the vein and remove it entirely. As for incision, this is the method: first bathe the leg well with warm water.
until the thick, turbid blood is loosened. Then tie the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then squeeze out the black blood with your hand from the lower leg upward and from above downward, until you remove the amount of blood you consider sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength can bear. Then bind it and tell him to avoid foods that produce black bile.  Repeat the purging and bloodletting.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient.  As for excision, this is the method: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair. Then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg with warm water until it reddens and sweats, or until he performs strenuous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, heat the limb. Then incise the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower end near the heel. Then open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from all sides until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it is dark red. When freed from the skin, it appears like a tendon. Then insert a probe under it until it is raised and emerges from the skin; hang it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then make another incision near that one, about three fingers wide.
Then dissect the skin from over the vein until it appears, then raise it with the probe as you did before and hang it with another hook as you did initially. Then make another incision, or many incisions if necessary.
Then dissect and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull and dissect it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when it has all emerged, you cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and dissection, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand to each side until it emerges. Take care that it does not break; for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to dissect it.
And some harm will come to the patient from it. When you have dissected it all, place wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or oil on the wounds, and treat it until it heals. If the varix is constricted, with convolutions and a twist in different directions,
and is not straight as we said, then it is necessary to make an incision on it at each part of its convolutions and places of twisting, then hang it with hooks until you dissect it completely. Take care when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for it will be difficult for you to dissect it, so be careful.
Image of the incision by which the varix is incised.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be curved like other hooks, nor should it have a sharp point, lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smooth, and curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the treatment of varicose veins.
