This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren lands with little fertility.  It may also be generated in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its generation is from an infection that occurs under the skin.
Just as living creatures, worms, and gourd-worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh occur inside bodies, the sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped in a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, and tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from its root before it all emerges, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing swelling, infection in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be carefully and gradually pulled out until it is completely removed and nothing of it remains in the body.  From this vein, in some people, there may emerge something five to ten cubits long.
And it has reached me that it emerged from a man to the length of twenty cubits. If it breaks for you while you are treating it, then insert a probe into its hole and probe it thoroughly along the body until all that is in it of matter is emptied, and then treat the place with medication for some days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, producing many openings, and from each opening a branch emerges. Treat it as we mentioned in the section and as previously described.
Chapter ninety-two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that is generated under the jaw and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease because it frequently affects cows. It is a single small worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is perceptible to the sense as it moves from one member to another until it perforates the skin at some point and emerges.  Its formation is from the putrefaction of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and gourd-like growths in the abdomen.
Its harm is expected to be that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and emerge, destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this can only be done when it is moving and perceptible to the sense.
It is necessary to tightly bind above and below it with a strong ligature, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you do not find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is its damage to the eye, as we have said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then bind tightly beneath it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it.  The patient should be treated with purgative medicines to cleanse the body of bad putrefied humors, and should abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the nafar.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafar*, is a pain that appears in some of the limbs, then moves from one limb to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She bared her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short time, I saw that swelling moving along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
As it moved from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a short while, it ran through the rest of her body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it travels around her whole body, as I witnessed.
I marveled at the speed of its movement from one limb to another.  I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen a number of people whose pain moved from one limb to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I judged that this was only because the woman was from the countryside, dry-bodied, and with visible veins; therefore, that moving wind was apparent to the senses. It should not appear in the same way in people of comfort and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels the pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it with a bandage and incise it until that congested wind emerges, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by purging the body and with things that dispel and disperse winds, such as the seeds of henbane and the seeds of squirting cucumber and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones; there are hollow glass ones and solid glass ones; and there are those with three points and those with four points.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either the organs are principal hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; if an arrow has lodged in one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I shall describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most instances. But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of an arrow wounding the brain and penetrating the bone and wounding the membrane which is upon the brain, there is intense headache, drowsiness and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of an arrow wounding the brain and penetrating the bone and wounding the membrane which is upon the brain, there is intense headache, drowsiness and dizziness. and redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, delirium, vomiting, and perhaps blood will come from the nostrils or ears; perhaps speech will be lost and the voice will be gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel will come from the place of the wound, and a watery liquid like the water of flesh will flow from it.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs if the arrow has struck the heart and is near the left breast, it will be felt as if it has been driven into something solid and not into something empty.
And perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat and fainting; then know that death is inevitable.  A sign of the arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels which are next to the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes; he breathes heavily and desires to inhale cold air. If the arrow has struck the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be labored with intense pain.
He will sigh and all the members of the shoulders will move. If the arrow has struck the liver, intense pain will follow, and blood resembling the liver in its redness will come from the wound. If the arrow has struck the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food will come from the wound.
And its condition is apparent. If the arrow has struck the abdomen and become embedded in it, and something of the faeces, or fat, or intestine has come out of the wound which has been ruptured, then there is no hope of its treatment or of extracting the arrow. If the arrow has struck the bladder and urine comes out,
and something protrudes from it outwardly, and the pain becomes intense for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for the other members, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are mostly unharmed.
This is so when the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned. And I will tell you some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows, so that you may thereby be guided in your treatment. That is that an arrow once struck a man in the canthus of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side, below the lobe of the ear, and he recovered, and no harm befell his eye.  And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow had become hidden, and I only reached the small end which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered and no harm befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
And it was one with barbs. So I made an incision for him between the jugular veins, for it had sunk into his throat. So I gently worked at it until I extracted it, and the Christian was saved and recovered. And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in the belly, and we judged that he would die from it.
But when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision over the arrow and skillfully worked at it and extracted it, and he recovered and no harm befell him.  And I saw a man whom an arrow had struck in the back, and the wound had healed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman whom an arrow had struck in her belly, and the wound had healed and the arrow remained, and nothing in her condition had changed, nor did she experience any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man whom an arrow had struck in the face, and the wound had healed and remained, causing him little pain. And there are many such cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man, one of the Sultan's guards, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right side.
And the whole arrow was hidden.  I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a slender probe, but I did not feel it. And he felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the point of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a dressing possessing drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrow's location would become apparent so that I could incise it.  But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that location.
So I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  Then the wound closed during that time, and the patient despaired of its removal for a while, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  Then I applied a strong corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it and felt the thin tip of the arrow which adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the arrow tip became clearly visible to us.  And it took me about four months with this.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to removal. I continued to gently manipulate it and devise ways with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with forceps, as its image is shown at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. And I tell you the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and example for those I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman measures the much from the little, and from what is present to what is absent, and devises a new operation and a new instrument for strange emergencies.
If we descend to this craft, then I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by pulling them from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction; those which are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, in which case it is pulled and removed. If it does not yield to removal at the time it is encountered, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh around it decays so that its extraction is easier.  This is also the case if it is embedded in bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, leave it also for some days and try again by pulling and moving it each day; then it will come out. If it does not yield to removal after several days, then you should bore around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine borer until you widen the opening for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow embedded in the skull has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms I mentioned appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after some days.
…then proceed to pull out the arrow. If it is very firmly embedded and does not yield to pulling, use the borers around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the wound until it heals. As for if the arrow has disappeared into a place in the body… …and is hidden from view, then explore it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some instrument suitable for pulling it out. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein…
…then incise it until the wound is widened and you can reach the arrow to extract it. If it has barbs, grasp them and carefully free the flesh caught in them from all sides by any means possible. If you cannot free the flesh, break off the barbs and twist them until you are free of them.
And when you attempt to extract the arrow from whichever place it may be, use the forceps in your hand, rotating it in all directions, to loosen it.  Be extremely gentle, lest the arrow break, making it difficult for you to pull and extract it. If you cannot manage it at once…
…leave it for some days until the flesh around it decays, then try again; it will then be easier. If you encounter bleeding, use the treatment mentioned in its chapter. Take great care not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon; use skill.
By any means you can, loosen the arrow; let this be done gently, slowly and steadily as I have described to you.  It is necessary that when you pull out the arrow, you place the patient in the same position he was in when the arrow struck him; this is more appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow that exits on the opposite side, either some of it will have emerged externally, or you will be able to feel the tip of the arrow just beneath the skin and see it protruding.
Then make an incision, the length of which should accommodate the forceps, and then pull it out; it will be easier to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, rotate your hand until the arrow affects the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and then try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead remains, push it with it. If the arrowhead has fallen off and you wish to use pushing, insert the hollow instrument such that its cavity enters the end of the arrow and then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that fits into that cavity; this will make the arrow easier to remove.  If the arrow is poisoned, you should, if possible, completely cut away all the flesh into which the poison has penetrated, and then treat it with an appropriate remedy.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, then make the incision, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like a bird's beak; they are made like a file so that when they grasp an arrow or anything else, they do not let go.  Large, small, and medium sizes are made, each according to the size of the arrow, the width of the wound, and its narrowness.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl to facilitate its entry into and pushing of the hollow arrow.
Chapter ninety-five.
On phlebotomy.
The veins which customarily are opened in the body number thirty, of which sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the "whispering" veins, and the two arteries in the temples which are apparent, and the two veins at the inner canthus of the eyes, known as the "seeing" veins.
And the vein erect in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip—these veins are known as the "veins of the throat"—and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins which are opened in the arm and hand, they are five veins; one of them is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median vein, which is the middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side, and is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears clearly above the thumb; and the salim.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the sciatic nerve, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of opening them is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic bad sores of the head, and the method of opening them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his head well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins appear and their place behind the ears in the two lower parts of the head, then you feel them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet-shaped knife, which is known as the *nashal*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the knife reaches the bone, then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin, a severed cut, and the length of the cut is the size of two joined fingers.
Or something like that, and you let out the amount of blood you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And I have already mentioned earlier in the book their cutting and cauterizing.  As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of opening them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And the method of opening them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulsation becomes apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the *nashal* lancet from below and raise the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need, then you loosen the patient's constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  I have already mentioned them, their cutting and cauterization, earlier in the book. As for opening the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after opening the vein of the nape, is for chronic facial ailments like ringworm, ulcers and erysipelas.
And the method of opening it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is at the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the amount you want, then you loosen the patient's constriction and bind it until it heals.
It may also be opened with a broad lancet, except that the lancet should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, but should be somewhat broad-ended, and you should open it obliquely, because the bone is near, and the lancet might break if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples, their benefit is for eye ailments such as ringworm, inflammation, and cataracts, and facial diseases. As for the method of opening them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you open them while you are standing over his head, and let the opening be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, somewhat broad lancet.
For there is no flesh in the location, for if the lancet is thin-pointed it might break.  Then you let out the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then you unbind them. As for opening the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases like erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The method of opening it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you grasp his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you insert it into the middle of the nasal septum itself, between the nasal partitions, straightly, because the vein does not appear to the touch there, for the blood will emerge immediately. And you should move your hand with the lancet a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it will congeal quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of opening them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, impetigo, bad ulcers, and cankers.  The method of opening them is that the patient constricts under them in his neck with a ligature, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you open the vein lengthwise, a slightly wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the ligature.
And you tie the veins with a moderate tightness, lest the patient suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the throat, the benefit of opening them, after opening the cephalic veins, is that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in the nose and around it.  The method of opening them is that you seat the patient before you and tie his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you ascertain them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins, so cut them with a cutting incision. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the largest and most evident ones.  Likewise, do this in the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of opening them after opening the cephalic veins is for the inflammations which are in the throat from the disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth. The method of opening them is that you seat the patient in your hands facing the sun, and you lift his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is towards blackness.
Then you open them and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps bleeding from those arteries will occur. As for the three veins which are opened in the elbow, they are those which custom has decreed to be opened in all people, and their opening is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, wind-shaped or olive-shaped lancet to the thinness, or by cutting with a scalpel, which is the lancet [and this is its image], the image of the wide, wind-shaped lancet:
It is wide, as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip, suitable for opening thin veins which contain thin, bilious blood. And this is the image of the lancet:
This is the lancet which is suitable for cutting, and there are wide and thin types of it, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And by this, one may infer the rest, and it is well-known among the artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen. It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when opening it, to beware and be careful, for beneath it is an artery. If he makes a mistake and goes too far in piercing with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur; therefore, it is necessary that its opening should not be done with a lancet for piercing, but its opening should be done by cutting with the lancet.  If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or you may open in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and cut it with the lancet as we said. If you wish to open it specifically, then before tightening the arm, it is necessary to feel the place until you identify the location of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and cut the vein with an oblique cut with the lancet, as we said, and take care that the stroke falls far from the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon opening the vein, spurt like a boy's urine, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops—and it often stops—then tighten the ligature.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on his back and not move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and overcomes you and you do not have a remedy at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly to the vein with a ligature and pledgets until the next day. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with what was previously mentioned of applying styptics, and stopping its bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature on the arm, inform him of it. As for the median vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the upper head and lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who opens it to be lying on his back when he opens it, for there is a nerve beneath it. If he goes too deep with the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness will occur in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. This nerve is often palpable.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not apparent, then you should make your incision with a lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for diseases of the eyes, and it is preferable in this vein especially, if you wish to open it, to make the incision with an olive-shaped lancet or with a broad, basil-shaped lancet because it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when bleeding, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft place. It does not harm if it is not hit with the first stroke to be bled again several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not bled on the first stroke.
But that swelling does not harm it in any way. As for the method of bleeding, its complications, and what should precede its treatment, the first thing you should know is that bleeding is either used only to preserve health.
Or it is used in illnesses.  That which is used to preserve health and prolong it and to guard against the occurrence of illnesses is that the bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median cubital, and the basilic, and that the bleeding should be at the beginning of spring if signs of fullness appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for bleeding which is used in illnesses, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need dictate it, whether by night or day.
And at every hour and at every time. Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years of age be bled. If someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever,
he should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained excrement within, lest putrid superfluities be drawn into the veins from the intestines during bleeding, harming the principal organs.  And one who is anxious, or drunk, or intoxicated should not be bled until that passes from them.
And bleeding should also be avoided after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then, look at the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
with foods, drinks, and medicines, if that is possible for him. Then, let him enter the bath, if nothing prevents him, or let him engage in some exercise so that the blood thins and make his bleeding in the middle of the day as we said, and let him aim to empty his chest that day of all bad psychological symptoms.
such as worries, anger, fear, and all physical symptoms like excessive exertion and effort, sexual intercourse, and the like. And let him prepare for his session the things that people customarily use, such as perfumes, fragrant herbs, entertainment, and the like, each person according to what he can manage.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then the patient extends his arm, and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the ligature with the fillet and twists it two or three times. Let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, which prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after the tightening, it is necessary for the patient to rub both his hands together.
until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then places the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself below the place he wants to bleed a little, lest the vein evade and avoid the incision. For some veins are like tendons, evading when being bled, and some are full of air, so when the lancet is placed on them, they sink beneath the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it does open it, it only opens it narrowly.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be certain and deliberate in all these matters, then lower the lancet. If the vein opens from that attempt, otherwise he repeats it again a little below or above that place quickly if the area is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is anxious, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may cause a hot swelling, and he should not enter the bath. Then he may resume bleeding if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet into the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do that quickly before the area swells, for in many people the area may swell with a small opening.
If you see it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Put something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this situation than oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac and the shakhzanājā may also do this if one of them is placed on the area, for the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the phlebotomy—and this often happens, especially to someone who has only been bled once or whose vein opening was small—then act quickly and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has completely exited the vein itself or from another vein. If something black or green remains in the area after some days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloes and myrrh that have been dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the plant *fudanj* and the like. Often a swelling and a boil occur with phlebotomy of the basil vein. Place your hand on it; if you find that it softens when you touch it, then it is a bad boil, so be careful not to apply anything of what we mentioned.
For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it, but bind it with something astringent to constrict the area, then treat it with the other treatments until it heals. And it is necessary to extract from each person a quantity of blood according to his strength and the color that is predominant in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red; and likewise, if you see that it is thick, let it flow until it thins; and likewise if it is acrid, until its acridity is gone. And it is necessary for someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed in one go...
...to widen the vein incision and the lancet should be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite should be done. And it is necessary that you extract it in several times, and that the opening be narrow. And the best thing to use in phlebotomy is something sharp, well-honed, incised, not pierced.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than phlebotomy across or lengthwise. And if someone is accustomed to fainting during phlebotomy, then it is necessary to feed him before phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is extracted from him three or four times, then if he has a cold temperament, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in hydromel or in honeyed syrup flavored with herbs or in a pleasantly-scented syrup.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the great loss of blood, then it is necessary to give him meat broth and thin, pleasantly-scented syrup; and he should be perfumed with galbanum, and his chest should be rubbed with it; and he should use everything else that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which is due to vomiting. And as for someone who wants to relax his arm and to let his blood flow again, it is necessary for someone whose phlebotomy was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak, that the blood be let little by little, according to his strength, on consecutive days.
And as for someone who wants to relax his arm and let his blood flow again, and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And as for someone who wants to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side from which it inclined, then it is necessary to relax him on the second or third day.
And as for someone whose blood is abundant, has become hot and sharp, and has caused fever, then it is necessary to extract the blood from him in a single session and to extract a large amount, and to widen the opening until fainting occurs, after you have checked all the conditions for phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, lest death occur instead of fainting; for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs.  And it is not necessary, if you want to loosen the arm and let the blood flow again.
And if the opening of the vein has closed and the exit of blood is difficult, do not prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you phlebotomize it again, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of blood in the opening of the vein.
Or apply something of salt dissolved in water, or apply something of theriac or  schechnaia, and prick it gently until the blood comes out.  If the vein is swollen, then leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity requires letting the blood flow again, then either phlebotomize above that place, or phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomizing the basilic vein, it is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic and basilic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature, slightly and moderately.
Then phlebotomize the vein at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide.  Your phlebotomy should be slightly above the joint of the hand. If the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water.  The use of hot water is most often done in winter. Phlebotomizing this vein is safest of all veins. Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand, after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand, after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. It becomes very perceptible to the touch. Then phlebotomize it at a slight angle. Even if you cut it completely, it will not harm him at all. Be cautious; do not plunge your hand with the lancet, for beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, and the location is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow. If you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the opening of the vein and cease flowing. When you have drawn the required amount of blood, apply ointment and salt to the vein, lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of phlebotomizing from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and you do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, the same. As for phlebotomizing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments.
And amenorrhea, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.  Its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and massages it until the veins become prominent. Then you tie above the joint of the leg with a ligature; and the vein is located at the heel, externally, towards the great toe.
And many branches extend from it on the front of the leg. Phlebotomize in its widest branch, or at the heel where it converges; this is best and safest.  If you phlebotomize on the front of the leg, be cautious of the nerves beneath it on the front of the leg.
And make your incision as if you intend to sever it, and let the lancet be sharp. If the blood does not flow, let him return his leg to the hot water and leave the blood to flow until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt,
let him try again a little higher, for the area is safe and nothing is to be feared if you are cautious of the nerve, as we said. Do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg. As for the sciatic vein, its location, as we said, is at the heel on the outer side.
And its benefit is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood.  Its method of phlebotomy is that you put the patient in a bath, hurry, and bind his leg from the hip to above the heel by four fingers' breadth with a thin, long bandage; for it does not appear except in this way.
Then, when it appears, phlebotomize it however you wish, either by incision, which is best, or by severing it or by splitting it; for its location is safe, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, phlebotomize some of its branches, which appear on the dorsum of the foot towards the little finger and ring finger, being careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you desire.
Then loosen the binding and place cotton on the site of phlebotomy and bind the area, for it quickly heals.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping instruments may be made of horn, wood, brass, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one with incision and evacuation of blood, and the other without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And cupping of the sides of the neck on both sides, and cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw, and cupping of the shoulders, and cupping of the coccyx at the base of the spine, and cupping of the forearms, which are the middle of the arms, and cupping of the legs, and cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the fine veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason it does not weaken the body as much as bloodletting, and cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any illness that results from fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If the need for cupping arises from illness or habit, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, or at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their heads; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and burning in their eyes; and some itch at the site of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood; and some sleep a lot; and some see blood, redness, slaughter, and wounds in their sleep, and things like that.  Whenever we see any of that, ...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water pouring into the eyes.
But it should be after evacuation of the whole body. This cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.  It is forbidden to use it for those who are cold-headed or have a cold, for it greatly harms them.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who overuse it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of forgetfulness being produced. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory organs, cough, and fullness. The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, because if it is lowered, it produces weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from headaches, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand with the lancet, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may serve as a substitute for phlebotomy of the jugular veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilic, cephalic, and median cephalic, because it attracts blood from all those small veins in the flesh and attracts those small veins from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
You should instruct the cupper not to go too deep with the lancet, because the area is sparsely fleshed and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping applied to the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
You should instruct the cupper that the cupping glass should be large and made of copper, because the area requires strong suction and a glass cupping glass may break and require many incisions. As for cupping the legs, it noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it attracts blood from the entire body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; and it benefits from boils and abscesses.  It serves as a substitute for phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the ankles, except that it weakens the body considerably and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping the ankles is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied. Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see that the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then make an incision and gently resume suction. Then observe the condition of the bodies.  If a person's flesh is thin and porous, then you should make only one incision, lest the area ulcerate.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision slightly and deepen it, and to adjust the suction gently and with a slight movement. If the blood is thick, then two incisions should be made: the first to open a path for the thin, watery blood, and the second to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a small amount of blood, we are content with one incision; if we want to extract a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we judge the blood to be thick.
Therefore, we should make a deep incision; the moderate limit of the incision is only the depth of the skin. What ointments, waters, and things should be avoided by the cupper when applying cupping glasses? As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
the areas for cupping should be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving ointments.  If it is summer, then use, for example, rose ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is winter, then use, for example, narcissus oil, iris oil, chamomile oil, lily oil, and the like. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram oil,  or balm oil, or benzoin oil, or rue oil, and the like.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These people should wash their cuppings after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or gooseberry water, or gourd water, or chickweed water, and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cuppings with vinegar, or with water of myrtle and sumac, and the like. As for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cuppings with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile, and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after the bathhouse.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bathhouse. No one should sleep after cupping. What the cupper and phlebotomist should manage before and after cupping.
First, it must be observed whether the cupper or phlebotomist is choleric, and if his blood is predominantly sharp and inflamed, then he should take cooling things like pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, sherbet, and the like.  He should make his foods of poultry and lamb stews, and sour things, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and oxymel, or seed oxymel, and he should consume moderately perfumed wine, which is between old and new. He should be ordered to eat little, and he should make his food of poultry, capons, birds, and young pigeons, stewed. The drink on the day of cupping and phlebotomy should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people at certain times some of the theriac of Avicenna, or musk medicine, or silphium before cupping and before phlebotomy, or after, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to those with fever.
As for cuppings which are without a condition, they are the cuppings which are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, the region of the kidneys, and the hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate the condition being applied to them. Rather, what is intended by them is either drawing blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in a case of epistaxis, or we use it to remove a cold humor which has gathered in an organ, such as placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it relaxes the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by resolving that humor.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if obstruction or stones appear in them, for by the strength of its drawing, it may perhaps open the obstruction or dislodge the stones from their place.  It does likewise if placed on the liver and spleen when a humor troubles them.  These cuppings may be used empty, only by suction.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation.  That is to fill the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the place, held, and removed, and repeated several times as needed. And this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth will be the width of two open fingers, as we have pictured, and its depth about half a span; and in its side, about the middle, there will be a small hole, about the size that a needle enters. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, thick-rimmed.
Smooth, level, polished, lest it harm the member when placed upon it; and in its middle there will be a crosswise groove of copper or iron, where the burning candle is placed.  And this cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the illnesses.
And the custom of its use is that the cupping glasses for boys and thin people are not the cupping glasses for men and those of robust bodies. As for the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is that you prepare a burning wick of tightly-woven linen or a small candle of pitch and place it on the middle of the hardened rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the ascent of the fire will be upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest it burn the body of the patient. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, until, when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then prepare the wick in the same manner, and replace it if you need to do so. As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of inflammation with water, it does not have a hardened rod or a hole.
But it is used by being filled with water and placed on the member only. And this is its image:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of smallness, like the lip and gum and so on, or because the member is bare of flesh, like the finger and nose and so on.
And the method of using them is that you should obtain leeches which are in fresh, clean water, free from impurities; then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and there remains nothing in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is applied; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to cup the place, this is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be moistened in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or alum or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply halves of peeled bitter almonds to the place.
And leave it until the almond adheres to the place, for then the bleeding will stop. And it is advisable, if it is necessary to reapply leeches, not to reapply those leeches if others are available. And if the leeches refuse to attach, let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or pierce a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply them; for when they sense some blood, they will attach themselves to the place. And if you wish to make them fall off, sprinkle on them some aloes, or salt, or ashes; then they will fall off in place.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also is from and concerning what is needed in the craft of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never consulted the books of the ancients in it, nor read a letter from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become nonexistent in our country, and indeed I have never encountered anyone at all skillful in it. And I have only derived what I have derived from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them, then I adhered to experimentation and practice for a long time.
And I have drawn for you from that in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has passed over it after I have brought it closer to you and freed it from the branches of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you in it many images of the instruments that are used in it.
For it is from the increase of explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Most Great.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and collections concerning the matter of fractured bones, which should be presented before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one.  It is necessary that we mention in the beginning of this chapter a collection of sayings and sections. It compels you, first, to understand them and stand upon their truth, you and others who are eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, it is necessary that you hasten first to bleed him or purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness, or the one to whom this has happened is a child or a very old man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his diet to cold vegetables, the meat of birds and gazelles; and forbid him drink, thick meats, and overfilling of the stomach, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and you do not expect the flow of material to the place.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, which was his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should be nourished with foods that nourish strongly, thickly, and solidly, which contain viscosity, like porridges, rice, heads, and necks, and cow tripe. And eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks and the like; for with this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and knit to their first nature.
Because of the dryness of their bones and their hardness. And it may unite and knit what was of bones in the extreme of softness, like the bones of small children. But nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick in it, which adheres to it and tightens it until some of it adheres to some of it.
And it binds some of it to some of it until it comes to the extreme of strength and solidity as it was at first, until nothing hinders it from its actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient food which contains solidity, viscosity, and thickness, as we said. And know that fractures differ in their types according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
And so all the other members, each is different from another. And we shall come to the mention of each type of fracture, explained in its chapter, detailed from another. And the type of bone fracture may also differ in itself.
For it may be its fracture a splintering without fragments occurring in it, and it may be its fracture along the length of the bone and its fracture has fragments and projections, separated and unseparated. And the fracture may be with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And of what makes known a fracture of the bone is its crookedness, its swelling, and its appearance to the touch, and its roughness when you probe it with your hand.
Then when there is not in the place an apparent crookedness, nor roughness, nor do you feel upon touching the bone a disturbance, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight crack.  Therefore, it should not be moved by pulling and probing at all.
Rather, apply to it of the medicines which the mention of will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is broken and split into two without fragments occurring in it, except that each part has moved from its companion.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to its straightening and alignment before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in whichever way you are able to with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned.  If the bone has fragments, then it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let your placing of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape, until when the broken part of the bone extends, then at that time return those fragments to their places in whichever way you are able to with skill and gentleness.
And exert yourself that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your action, and exert yourself to join one of the bones to its companion in the best manner. It is necessary at that time to touch and feel them with your hand; if you see there something contrary, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, and they frequently cause by their actions a hot swelling or stiffness in the limb, as I have witnessed repeatedly from their actions.
Then, after the setting, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking hours and sleep, and during his turning and restlessness, and during his defecation and all his movements to the best of his ability, and that he should strive to have the position of the limb in a position that ensures safety from pain.
And that is because, whenever he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and he should also strive to make that position level and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the limb when it is healed.  As for the manner of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and forms; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar.
Then it is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle; and those that are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be wide and firm, because a wide bandage is necessary for a large limb and tightens it from every side with equal tightness so that no defect intervenes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. In a soft, pliable manner on the place of the fracture; then you begin to wrap the bandage on the place of the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the limb requires, and tighten your hand a little with the bandage, then take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tightening than your tightening of the broken place, then you gradually spread the wrapping away from the place of the fracture a little and loosen the tightening little by little until you take something suitable from the correct place, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
wraps; then take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap; then place between the bandages from the soft pliable material or rags what straightens the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it; then wrap another bandage around it, then set on these bandages the firm splints at once if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what soothes that swelling and removes the inflammation, and leave it for some days, then tighten the splints on it at that time; and let the splints be from wide, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or palm leaflets or Arundo donax or  boxwood and the like of what is available, and let the width of each splint be in this way and this shape itself.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb, large or small.  Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage, in the same way as your first tightening, then bind from above with strong threads, according to what we mentioned concerning the tightening, which is that your tightening should be more on the place of the fracture. And the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be of wet linen especially, for if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them is excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable, because you will not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger's breadth between the splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the healthy areas, place soft padding or fluffy wool underneath so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture occurs in the skin, we will mention that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day.  That is, if the limb is large, the splints should not be applied until after five or seven days, or more, depending on your assurance that hot swelling will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and wounds. A description of a common, brief bandage which sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not excessively hot or cold.
It is that you take mill dust, which is the finest flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the grinding process, and knead it as it is, without sifting it, with egg white, making the kneading neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
The description of another bandage which sets fractures, dislocations, and wounds: take of mastic, leadwort, acacia,  madder, tragacanth, and gum arabic, ten drachms of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and of  athel, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything together and mix with the water of athel, or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. Then use this bandage, for it is well-composed, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The description of another bandage which is also useful for fractures and wounds: take tragacanth, mastic, and white lead, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white, and use it.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones dislocated from their places, which soothes the accompanying pain, and for bone fractures and breaks: take carded wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the area.  This bandage has no bone-setting power.
But it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pain in particular. The description of another bandage which sets broken bones: take the leaves of the deaf fig and the leaves of wild poppy, pound them together, and bandage with them while moist. The description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is being set.
And you want to resolve the remainder of the swelling, take of the root of white lead, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed barley, one part of each; pound everything together and knead it with egg white if the member is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or water, and use it.
The description of another bandage stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when setting a bone: take of the root of white lead, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, germander, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead it with vinegar water, or with fresh water, or with egg white, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its inflammation.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when setting it.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things which contain gums with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and similar things that we mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is being set.
The description of a bandage that benefits weakness and pain: take myrrh, alum, human hair that has been cut or bird feathers, white lead, and salt, equal parts; pound, sift, and knead, and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain.
Then it is loosened; that is to say, you should look; if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the affected area, do not loosen it for many days; but if severe itching, troublesome pain, or swelling occurs in the place.
Then hasten to loosen it immediately and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft rag or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside. Then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bandage it for a night, until you are safe from hot swelling, the swelling of the member subsides, and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as tightly as the first time.
And be gentle with it until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have completely gone, and you need a bandage and tightening, then prepare it as you did at first, the same. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member.
Then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you of the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh begins to knit over it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the richness of the patient's food to what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried out and wasted more than it should, then know that food is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and lessen the tightening a little. For by this action, nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the member again,
if it is not set properly at first, and it is set crookedly, this is a mistake in their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either sever the bone completely until it reaches the membrane under the bone. Just as the wedge does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called wedge-shaped.  Or, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating to the end; this type of fracture is called a complete avulsion. The wound in both of these fractures may be either large or small.
And some fractures are comminuted or depressed; their cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may either penetrate nearly to the membrane under the bone, or it may be only on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two fractures is also either large or small. And some fractures are hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair, being a slight fissure.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  And there is a fracture which results from a fall or a blow with a stone or the like; it drives a section of the bone inwards, and the location becomes concave, as happens to a copper vessel when struck, so that a part of it is driven inwards.
This occurs most often in the heads of young children, where the bone is soft. All these types of fractures may have loose and non-loose fragments. We will describe the treatment of all of them in their proper place, and we will identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is known by examining the bone, wiping it, and smearing it with ink; then the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first look to the symptoms of the patient.
If you see among his symptoms that which clearly indicates fear, such as vomiting bile, distension, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, protrusion and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient nor treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his safety, then begin his treatment.  And if the wounded man comes to you at the outset of his injury, and this is in the winter months,
then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. And if it is in the summer months, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone is corrupted, and those symptoms that we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and bruising, then you should cut away the crushed and bruised part as I describe to you; and that is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whichever way is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when uncovering the bone, you find bleeding or hot swelling, then treat that as is appropriate.
And that is to pack the area with cloths soaked in a potion and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to chip away at the bone and remove it; this can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a delicate saw with a narrow blade, and this is its image:
Then you use another saw after this one, slightly wider; and this is its image:
Then you also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have a number of different saws, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, and that they be extremely sharp at their edges, and let them be of Indian steel or good tempered steel.
And use gentleness in applying the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, then it is necessary that you pierce around it before using the saws, with the drills which they call non-immersive drills; and indeed they are called non-immersive drills.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is beyond it, because the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a small ring or circle, preventing it from penetrating and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take a large number of these drills, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you will have for every skull a drill whose sharp point is in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull. And this is an image of three types. of the drills: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of drilling around the fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another place, and you make the distance between each hole the amount of the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then cut with the cutters between each two holes, and do that with the utmost gentleness you are capable of, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments that you have prepared for that.
such as forceps and gentle hooks. And it is necessary that you take the utmost care that the drill or cutter does not touch anything of the peritoneum. Then, if you have removed the bone and it is free from the peritoneum, if it was adhering to it, and you have taken it out, then it is necessary to cleanse it.
And smooth the roughness of what remains in the bone with another instrument resembling the cutter, except that it should be thinner and gentler than the other cutters.  If anything remains of the small bones and fragments, then take them gently with what you have of instruments.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with cauterization and ointments, the mention of which will come. As for the other method of working, it is a very easy method, far from danger, Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly. And this is what he said. It is necessary for you first to begin by uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and evident, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the tip of this lenticular cutter, and this is its image:.
Its lenticular part will be smooth, not cutting anything, and its sharp part will be on its sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutter on the bone, then you strike the cutter from one side.
