With a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane, nothing frightening happening in it at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, then scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular cutter itself and carefully free it from it, for it will be freed from it without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken of the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then it is necessary to remove that roughness and extract those fragments with delicate forceps, of which several sizes have been made, so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate one in each place, according to what the work leads you to.
And the same broken bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you use first in your scraping of the bone the widest of those forceps, then use after it a finer one, and continue to do that successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like fissures and slight fractures, it is necessary to use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing; this is something not hidden from anyone who has the least experience in this art and has understood what we have written and explained in large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, then you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And soak it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can so as not to burden the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain that the hot swelling has subsided, then remove it and use afterwards medicines that have a drying effect, such as the roots of iris, crushed gallnuts, crushed myrrh, and zedoary, and the like.  This is done by making plasters from these medicines.
And sprinkle them on the wound as they are dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses and does not sting, and strive during your treatment of the wound that it be clean, free of oil residue or dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing great harm to the patient.  This may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
Blackness on its surface is observed. If the patient shows the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he will inevitably perish.  However, if the blackness resulted from a medicine placed on it, and it was in the nature of that medicine to cause that blackness.
Then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and mix them well, and spread them on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the various appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the reduction of a broken nose.
Know that only the upper two parts of the nose, or one of them, can be broken, because they are bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break; rather, contusion, crookedness, and flattening occur to it.
If one of its parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten that fracture from the inside with your index and thumb from the outside, until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done gently by you, being careful not to cause the patient pain by your actions.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be straightened with the end of a probe of slight thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise.  And let it be treated on the first day of the fracture.
If possible; otherwise, after the seventh or tenth day, when the hot swelling has subsided, then insert a tent of linen cloth into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or insert two tents if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the tents be of a thickness to fill the nostril. Some early bone-setters mentioned that the tents should be moistened with fat and changed every day. I do not think this is so; rather, the tents should be moistened with egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the tents until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened. Goose quills, after being wrapped with soft linen cloth, may be inserted into the nose at the location of the tents; this will make the retention of the nasal fracture firmer and will prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; you may do it or you may make tents. If hot swelling occurs in the nose during your work, apply a compress to the nose with  kypros or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or something of diachylon ointment.
If hot swelling does not occur, you should dress it externally with fine semolina and fine frankincense mixed with egg white, and then place a soft compress over it. Do not bind the nose at all if the nasal bones are fractured slightly.
Or if they are shattered, you should make an incision over them and remove them with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments that promote healing and closure.  If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with tents and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of lower jaw fractures.
If the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and not broken into two parts, and is not depressed inwards, then its identification is easy.
If the fracture is in the right side, then the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, then the index finger of the right hand should be inserted, and the elevation of the fracture should be gently raised from the inside outwards.
With your other hand from outside the bone, you control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has been broken into two, then it should be manipulated from both sides in a straight line until it can be straightened. If it has affected the teeth, they will loosen.
Or if they separate, then tie what you hope will remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a compress on the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth on top of it, and place on the cloth a firm large splint or a piece of leather from a sole equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it from above according to how you can best tie it and how it matches the setting, so that it does not come undone. Order the patient to be patient and still, and give him soft liquid food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to undo it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to how you can best tie it and how it matches the setting, so that it does not come undone. Order the patient to be patient and still, and give him soft liquid food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to undo it on the third day. Then you may repair what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or fine semolina after removing the compress, and place a soft pad on the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not untie it until it heals and the fracture solidifies. Often this fracture solidifies in three weeks. If a hot inflammation occurs during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to soothe it until that inflammation subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, look: if a fragment or fragments of bone have become detached, then gently remove those fragments with whatever instrument suits you for their removal. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as you need.
Then, when you have removed those fragments and none remain, stitch the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in the front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture is of one of three types: either it is broken into two without splintering, which is easiest to set;
or splinters are produced in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound. The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you should prepare two assistants, one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then you set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither projection nor irregularity. If you need more extension, you should place under the armpit of the patient a ball of rags or wool.
Its size should be according to your need. You extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
And a moderately sized cushion is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle which is deep rises upwards. Then you set the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken and is moving, you should make an incision upon it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, you should try to cut it with one of the cutting instruments which you have prepared for that purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument which supports the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a curve.  Its width should be according to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length should also be according to what is possible for the work. Let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound which you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are sure that there is no inflammation, then join the edges of the wound with sutures. If the wound is small or you fear inflammation, then dress the wound with lint and bandages to the extent of the incision.
If inflammation occurs, moisten the lint with rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone which is without a wound or incision, you should apply to the bone a plaster made from mill dust and egg white. And you place soft toweling on it, then you place a ball under his armpit if he needs it.  Then take a bandage which is very long, and its width is a cubit or so, then apply a folded bandage over the toweling and the plaster, then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise. Then wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth together with the splint in the part of the bandage which lies on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as it goes around. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit, and pass it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the point of all this is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then let the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm may be raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture will be raised by raising the shoulder; or tie his arm to his neck. And do not untie the bandage unless something happens at the site, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. And leave it bandaged until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it will become stronger in twenty-eight days. And it may be in less time for some people.
Chapter Six.
On setting a broken shoulder.  The shoulder rarely breaks in its wide part; rather, its edges break.  When a part of it breaks, or it breaks in its middle, this is known by touch, and the treatment varies according to the nature of the fracture.
Straighten it and restore it to its natural shape by any means you can, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft tow, and place on top of this a folded cloth pad. Then apply a wide splint of wood.
A splint as wide as the whole shoulder, or a little wider. If, under the splint, there is a roughness in any part of the shoulder, smooth out that roughness with soft tow until the splint lies evenly, then bind it above with a long bandage.
Bind it firmly and ensure that the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage each day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. A shoulder injury heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this period is complete, remove the bandage safely, for it is among the bones which are not feared for shattering or displacement. If a splinter of bone protrudes and is irritating under the skin, then incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the treatment of a clavicle fracture to alleviate the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, though this rarely occurs; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there occurs severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood is expelled and the depression in the fractured bone is evident and not hidden from touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are compressed and the ribs are drawn together by hands from both sides, and gentleness is used in straightening it in every way possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be, then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of thin wood from willow or elm or something similar that is light, after wrapping it in cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it shift, and pass the bandage around to the back repeatedly and tighten it firmly.  Then examine the bandage frequently, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal because of suppuration that occurs in the area, or pain, or swelling, then hasten to remove it and take off the bandage, and treat what has occurred according to its treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And face it and maintain firm pressure until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the treatment of fractured ribs. Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts near the back, and their ends in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous.  The knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense when examined with the fingers.
And its treatment is to set the fracture with the fingers in the accessible way until the shape is even as it should be. Then bandage and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs it. If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then severe pain occurs to the patient.
And it sinks like the sinking which occurs to one with a lung puncture, because the bone penetrates the diaphragm.  There also occurs for him shortness of breath, coughing, and the expulsion of much blood. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many methods for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient food which produces flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen swells and expands, and the fracture is driven outwards. And we dislike this lest it strengthens the occurrence of hot swelling if it has not occurred.  If it has occurred, then it increases it and strengthens it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then strongly sucked. This is more similar to the correct approach, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the place in its weakened state. And some of them said that the place should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And a bandage is made between the ribs until it is full, so that the binding is even when it is wrapped around. Then the patient is treated with the treatment of a lung puncture, in terms of diet and medicine, if the patient is overwhelmed by a severe matter that he cannot bear. And the bone had penetrated the diaphragm in a harmful way, and we feared for the patient.  It is necessary that we incise the place and uncover the fractured rib, then we place under it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching and we treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this, then act quickly: wet bandages in rose oil, place them on the place, and treat the patient with what relieves the swelling from the inside as well.
And he lies on the side on which sleep comes easily to him until he is healed.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens to them, and what happens more often is contusion—and similarly the vertebrae of the back also, if this happens to someone and you wish to know whether it will heal or not. Then look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed and numb and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has been spared and that the patient will recover with treatment.  If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you wish to know whether it will heal or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and faeces are passed involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine is passed involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is doomed; do not trouble yourself with his treatment.  If none of this occurs to him, the matter is less serious. The treatment of what has happened is to try to relieve the hot swelling.
By placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the area one of the strengthening, drying compresses, and bind it tightly, and order him to remain still and quiet.
And let him sleep only on the side where he feels no pain until he recovers. If, when the bone was injured, a splinter or something has been broken off from it, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the rump bone, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
And set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as you are able and as the setting allows, then apply a compress and splint to it, if that is needed, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in that their extremities are shattered, they split lengthwise, and they incline inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the area, numbness, and a loss of feeling in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only in the extremities, then set that fracture as well as you can until it resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you may set the fracture. When you have set it, apply a dressing and then place a splint of wood or leather upon it and bind it tightly.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or the splint coming loose. Fill in any hollows in the hips with padding so that the binding is even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his healthy side. If there is hot swelling,
desist from setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what will soothe it as described above, then return to setting and binding it properly. If there are splinters or fragments of bone, do not attempt to remove them.
They should not be touched but smoothed from the outside as we have said, and the binding should be left in place until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, it is set in one of two ways: one is to take a smooth, curved piece of wood of moderate thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the wood, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with both hands until he returns the fracture to what is appropriate. The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you want the stretching to be stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches them to his side.
If the fracture is near the end of the shoulder, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture is set gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bind it if no hot swelling appears. If hot swelling appears, then leave binding it until the seventh day, and place on it soft wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bind it. The method of binding is to place the bandage on the fracture, then place a bandage of new cloth over the bandage, then place the arm on the upper arm itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and binding to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something will change, then use splints.
This is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint.  And let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your tightening on the place of the fracture be stronger.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see that the placement of the splints and the tightening, as we said, is appropriate at the time of your setting the limb, then do so.
And if you fear hot swelling, leave off the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur in the place chafing or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightening.  Then correct all that according to what we have mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of that will happen, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back and his hand on his stomach, and a cushion filled with wool, moderately full, be placed under the arm.
And examine at every time, night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disordered or the bandage become loose.  Then correct all that with your effort.  And make the patient's diet of the kind we presented earlier, that the food be light at first, until the bone is about to consolidate.
Then it is necessary to make his food more substantial. For it is the nature of the arm and leg to consolidate in forty days. Then it is necessary to loosen [the bandages], and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments suitable for that. If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna; one is small, which is that next to the thumb, and the other is large, which is situated under the small one from below.  So perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps both are broken together.
When the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. When the lower ulna is broken, its fracture is bad and its healing difficult. And the worst is if both bones are broken. If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
the physician should, when setting it, make its extension gentle and easy until he straightens it. If the larger ulna is broken, then he should make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are broken, then he should make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be placed stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient should sit squarely upon himself.
And let the cushion be at a suitable height for him, so that the patient does not suffer hardship.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he restores it to the best shape he can. If there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place to the best of your ability. If a fragment appears that is separated and is piercing the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then make an incision upon it and extract it in the manner that we have previously described. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then this has been dealt with separately in a chapter.
Then take the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a compress of kyrotos (prepared with rose oil and white wax). Let it be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.  Bind the compress gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the kyrotos and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints. Let the splint that is placed upon the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one radius or in both radii, then make your binding at the site of the fracture stronger and tighter. And whenever you turn the binding upward or downward, make the binding slightly looser, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the compresses that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of fine linen, of medium thickness as we have described. And check the limb and bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be bathed with warm water until that itching subsides.  And the limb should be left unbound for a night so that it may rest, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then correct all of that to the best of your ability. And look also, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, then it should be loosened slightly and left for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. He should be careful to avoid restless movements, and let him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that depends on the conditions of the temperaments and the conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the palm and fingers.
Fractures of the metacarpus and phalanges rarely occur, but contusions occur frequently.  Therefore, when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand…
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair directly in front, level with him.
Then he should place his hand upon it, extended. Then the surgeon should extend the broken bones and set them, until they are well-aligned. Then, if there is no hot swelling, a bandage and a splint should be applied; then a further splint should be applied over the top, according to the location.
And it has been placed in a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm, make a kind of ball from a cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then tighten it with a long cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather, so that it conforms to the skin with the flexion of the entire hand, and it should be tightened appropriately. If the fracture is to the outside, a splint should be placed on top and another below in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight.
Then the bandage should be applied as the hand rotates, and the fingers should be interwoven with the bandage. If the fracture affects one of the finger phalanges, if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary and then bandaged with the hand; and if you wish, you may make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it should not move. If the fracture is to the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, they should be straightened and bound with the adjacent healthy finger, or all bound together, which is better, or a small, upright splint should be placed on them.
As we said concerning the thumb, and examine during and after your setting for hot swelling; counteract it appropriately whenever such a thing occurs, as described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone frequently breaks and is apparent to the touch, because it moves forward and backward.  Its treatment is to tightly bind a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below it, the patient lying on his stomach.
Then each bandage is extended to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; but if it is near the head of the femur, then it is necessary to bind a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material.
At the head of the femur towards the pubis, so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to maintain the bandage and binding, if no hot inflammation occurs in the limb. But if inflammation occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for binding it, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide bandage around the fracture two or three times, leaving some remaining. Then the leg is twisted until the heel is at the origin of the buttock, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, the ends of the thread being above on both sides.
Then wrap around the leg and thigh what remains of the bandage. Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint on the leg bone. Then stuff the space between the thigh and leg with soft cloths so that the binding is even.
Then begin strong binding from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns; and the farther the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less, softer, and looser should be your binding.
Then take the ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it the adjacent splints from above; then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and with them also tie the ends of the splints from the other side.
Lest the bandage should shift from its place. Then leave the binding as it is as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, and the like, occurs in the limb. If any of that happens, hasten to unbind it and remedy whatever has occurred, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding splinter in the bone, it should be set, if possible; otherwise, incise over it and extract it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.  This fracture of the thigh may be bound without the leg being included in the splints.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that its setting in this way does not result in lameness for the patient. But if it is set alone without including the leg, then the patient will inevitably limp forever.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less; all this according to the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap is rare, but it is often contused. If it is fractured, it will be either a fissure or a shattering into fragments, and this occurs with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and its reduction is to align the separated fragments with the fingers until they are joined and united, as far as accuracy, gentleness and skill allow.  Then apply a dressing and a circular splint if necessary.
Then tighten the bandage appropriately, and treat all the conditions we described for other fractures, such as acute inflammation, etc., by countering each symptom with the appropriate remedy until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the reduction of leg fractures.
The leg has two bones; one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg will be displaced in all directions; if only the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will be displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this happens lower down, it is something that will not escape your notice.
So use traction and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and you should take great care in its treatment.
And in treating the leg there is something additional to the treatment of the arm: when you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two thin pieces of pine wood, of the kind used in plastering rooms, which are placed between the cracks in the planks, or [take pieces] from palm tree stalks or similar material.
Choose pieces of moderate thickness, not too thin, and let their length be that of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one twice around its length with a cloth, and place one on each side of the leg.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two pieces of wood in three places: at the ends and in the middle. By this binding, the leg will be prevented from tilting to either side, and will be well supported.  A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and it is most necessary to do this in fractures accompanied by a wound, especially. Then examine the leg every day and give it meticulous care regarding swelling, inflammation, or anything else. If any of these things occur, treat them appropriately until healing is complete.  A leg bone mends in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fracture of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, fractures never occur there; as for the bones of the leg, fractures may occur there, and as for the toes, fractures rarely occur, but contusions are more common.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the leg and you see that some of those bones have overlapped, let the patient place his foot flat on the ground as if walking, then you stand and place your foot on the elevated part of those bones, then press down and set them.
until they return to their places, then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloths and everything else it needs.
If three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage; you will find the bones straight, whether they were broken or dislocated.  The procedure is as you see. As for if some of the toes are broken, set them and straighten them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken toe along the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider than it, then place under the foot the board that I described to you and bind it tightly. If what was broken of the bones was two or three or more,
then make for each toe a splint of appropriate size, layered in soft cloth, and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot; and let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to hold it firmly in place.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures, from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom as it should be.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently bend her backward little by little, and let her be held from behind. Then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her loins are raised little by little; then that cotton comes out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone returns. Then a compress is placed on her back. When she wants to urinate, the cotton is removed gently so that she can urinate.
And it is returned in the manner in which it was first inserted, then she returns to her first compress. She does this for seven days or so, then it heals. And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow strongly into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; then the fracture returns. Then pack it with cotton and leave it as we described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this is used in its treatment.
And its setting is as we described for the hip bone. The correct treatment of these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone who has some experience and understands this book well, is not hidden from you, for the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
Understand the correct way to treat and bind it. As for the penis, if it is fractured, take a goose's windpipe and insert the penis into it; then a bandage of cloth is placed on it, and it is bound and left for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the treatment of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or humerus or similar, should immediately be bled at the time if the conditions for bleeding are present, as we have previously explained.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then, set the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If no hot inflammation occurs, then if hot inflammation does occur, delay setting it until the ninth day until the hot inflammation subsides, and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it exposes it to bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should gently try to reduce and align it with your hands with a slight pull. If you cannot reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument; it is an instrument made of iron, its length being about seven or eight fingers. And its width is according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs in the treatment of each type of fracture.  Let them be round, with a little thickness, so that they do not bend when pressed during use.
And let the ends be sharp, with a curve at its end. Let the top be thicker and from its middle to the bottom much thinner.  This is its image:
It is called in Greek *pyrmos*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone, and it should be pushed gently until the bone returns and is somewhat aligned, so that you align the ends of the fracture with one another.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reset the bone as we have described at all, then cut it with a similar cut to those we have mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can. Then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, bothersome pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed the setting of the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment or anything containing fat to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then, when you have finished setting the bone, use splints and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And beware, with all caution, of tightening the bandage around a fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing either death, gangrene, or tetanus in their patients. Let your binding be gentle, loose, and unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the place, then you should not apply splints, but make bandages from strong cloths in the place of the splints, and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the drink, then use lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and the like, and you should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals.  It is necessary to position the limb so that the pus flows downwards easily. If many days pass and the wound does not heal and the pus does not cease, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
Then you should probe the wound; whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract it; and whatever is not loose, and is causing pain and injuring the limb, then work at cutting and extracting it in every way you can.
If the wound shows signs of gangrene, or ulceration, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should counter each symptom with a similar treatment to that which was previously described in its chapter.
And what you should pay attention to and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the femur or humerus and similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull it or extract it. For death often results from this; rather, leave it until it putrefies, for perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications which occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints, making the limb unsightly and perhaps preventing its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is fresh, use astringent medicines such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, gum tragacanth, acacia, and similar things. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage undisturbed for many days, then remove it and reapply another, until the complication disappears, God willing, or bind a tight lead plate to it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and removal is necessary, incise it from above and cut away the superfluous growth, or scrape it off with a scalpel until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it has healed but the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has healed but the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One cause is excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages, or excessive tightening of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive and repeated bandaging, or excessive movement at the wrong time, or a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases, and to use the bath, and to make him happy and cheerful, and so on. Then, apply pitch to the limb so that it draws much nourishment to it, or repeatedly bandage it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured. Unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, it is not proper to accept the opinion of one who claims to break the limb from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country used to do.
And this action is utterly reprehensible, leading to great danger, the least of which is injury; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, it should be treated with water in which emollient herbs, such as mallow leaves and root, and vervain and the like, have been boiled.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages, such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and cerate, and bandaged with it, or fat figs should be pounded with pigeon droppings and the like, among those medicaments called anti-adhesive.
And sometimes the complication will be resolved by constant gentle rubbing with the hands, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times should be employed; but if the crookedness is old and severe and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it should be incised above, and the connection of the bone released, and whatever remains of the complication or bone should be cut with fine cuts, and gentleness should be employed therein with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
Dislocation of the jaw is the displacement of a joint from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the patient severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, if anyone suffers a dislocation of the jaw, he should immediately proceed to its reduction.
He should not delay at all, for if he delays, the area will swell and become inflamed, making reduction of the jaw difficult. Therefore, he should not delay, nor move, nor stretch it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient cramping and harmful pain.
But if this occurs, he should immediately bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then he should bathe the limb with warm water and oil, then gently reduce it, and treat each limb as described in its place.
The chapters on the jaw have also been arranged, as with fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
Rarely do both jaws become dislocated; their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced, becoming somewhat relaxed, or they are completely dislocated, becoming relaxed. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot hold it back, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stutters when he speaks. But if the dislocation is slight, in most cases it will return of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its reduction quickly and not delay it at all.  This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head, while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands from the outside, with which he adjusts it, and he instructs the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every direction, and the physician adjusts the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, then apply warm water and oil compresses until their reduction is facilitated, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said.  When they have returned and become straight, and the patient's mouth is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply to them a bandage of rags with a poultice made from wax, oil and rosewater.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left, and he should not strain to chew anything, but his food should be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, then let him eat whatever he wishes, and let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, and yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they become dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and the patient's abdomen may become distended, and he may vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it has become corrupted.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and the point of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not become dislocated from the inner side because of its connection to the chest; it may become dislocated to the outside, and this is apparent to the touch.  Its reduction is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the place with your hand strongly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and supports and bind it tightly. As for its end which is near the shoulder and connected to it, it is rarely dislocated; but if it is dislocated, it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as seems best to you.
Then apply a bandage and supports and bind it tightly, and instruct the patient to remain quiet and still until it heals. With this same treatment, the point of the shoulder is also reduced if it has also shifted from its place.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder is dislocated in three ways: one is that it is dislocated towards the armpit downwards; the second is that it is dislocated towards the chest; and it may be dislocated above the shoulder, but this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the rear because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve.  Most often, it dislocates and moves downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, because it quickly comes out and quickly goes in.
But as for those who have much flesh, it is otherwise, that is to say, it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  And it may happen to some people that because of a blow or a fall, the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, and they think that it has dislocated; it is necessary to examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment.  The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit: if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the humerus twisted; and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the humerus is like an egg.
And the patient cannot raise his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find this apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things which relax and soften, such as cooking the root of mallow, fenugreek and rosemary in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and firmness. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its place. And if you wish to reduce it in this way, you should bring a taller man than the patient and have him stand at the side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raise his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his stomach. If the patient is light, it is necessary to hang something else on him to weigh him down, for then the jaw returns immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, the head of which is round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient stands on the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and he also extends his body from the other side forcefully, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers, and its thickness is about two fingers, and it has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient, then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal beam. Let it be horizontal, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side; then the joint enters immediately. If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates from above. Then the ball is firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wound from above, and his hand is hung from his neck.
And he is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be little until the limb is strengthened, for this is faster for its healing. Then it is untied after seven or five days, and it is exercised with movement; if it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another cause, then cauterization with three cautery irons should be used as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and its tightening are repeated many times, and it is not stable, and it falls and relaxes, and she cannot raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder is severed, or stretched, or relaxed. Then know that the joint will never be stable in its place.
As for the dislocation which is towards the chest and breast and towards the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, and all the tightening and treatment is used until it heals. If, after healing, numbness appears in the limb and it is slow in movement,
let the patient use the bath repeatedly until that numbness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty also. It is dislocated in all directions, especially forwards or backwards, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is under sight and touch, to whichever direction it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be clearly apparent to you by the depression of the joint, and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it. And you should hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot inflammation occurs to it.
For if a hot inflammation occurs to it, its reduction is difficult and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards, for that is the worst of all types of dislocation and the most painful, and death often follows it.
And its reduction, if it is of a kind that can be reduced, is that a servant extends his hand with both his hands and arm extended, and the physician's hands are above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands or with the base of his palms until it returns to its place.
And as for if the dislocation is forwards, it may be reduced by bending the hand forcibly until the base of its palm strikes the corresponding shoulder; and if the dislocation does not return, use very strong extension, which is that two servants extend the arm.
And two servants also hold the patient so that he does not move during the extension, then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand. And when the physician touches the joint with his hands, he should lubricate them with oil so that it helps in easily slipping the joint.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns; and after its return, a bandage containing astringents and desiccants, along with egg white, should be applied tightly, and the arm is suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied; and if the joint is stable in its place, untie the bandage and leave it. And if you see that the joint is not strengthened well, reapply the bandage and leave it for several days until it strengthens, then untie it.  And if a swelling occurs after the reduction and untying of the joint,
and difficulty in movement occurs, then use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and rubbing until it softens, or place a poultice of fat mutton on the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and night, then remove it and take him to the bath; and when he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the poultice once, twice, and three times, along with going to the bath, until it softens. And if you wish to apply a moist, light poultice of cow dung with fat and bind it on, do that several times, for it softens and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easy unlike other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it dislocates, before the place swells or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board, and let an attendant extend his hand; and let the physician place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns.  However, it should be examined whether the dislocation has gone towards the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let the palm of his hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that alleviates the swelling.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate [further manipulation], and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint remains crooked and does not harm the patient in any way except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve is severed or bruised; there is no remedy for it except cauterization, which may or may not be helpful. When the reduction of the wrist is complete, apply the bandage we described, then bind it tightly and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to place. Then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight on that day. If it is nighttime, bind it in the same manner; continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death quickly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excrement passes involuntarily; he cannot retain it, and often some of his limbs relax, whether his legs, or arms, or one of them. But if only one vertebra is displaced, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement is in four directions; that which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should observe: if the hump has occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, and no cure at all. But that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or the like...
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are of no benefit. I have shortened this, making the little that remains sufficient for the much that they produced, by my approximation to the meaning and explanation of it; and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
I say that a hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure; and so also that which is to the sides.  Only that which occurs in the back is treated, in the way that I describe. This is to stretch the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet mat beneath him so that his chest is not hurt. Then place an upright plank fixed in a hole in the ground at the head end of the bench, and another plank towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the plank—let it not be firmly fixed in the hole—and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his heels. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other plank at his feet. Then each servant pulls on his plank with the bandages, and the two planks do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not firmly fixed, as we said.  The physician places his hands forcefully on the hump until it returns, or he places a board on it, then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits long.
and make a hole in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly so that he presses down on the hump.
and it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-twisted screw, then you plant two pieces of wood, a cubit long, in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two pieces of wood.
Let the distance between each piece of wood be a span.  And a hole is made in each piece of wood through which the screw passes.  And all the wood is firmly secured in the ground so that it does not move at all.  And insert a round rod, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which a rod a span long is firmly fixed with what is twisted. And in the other two pieces of wood similarly. Then tighten the bandage which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw which is at his head, and the bandages which you tightened on his legs in the screw which is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the instrument with which he twists the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have previously explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying compress with egg white, then lint, then place on top of the compress a splint of wood, its width three fingers or so, and its length as much as takes the place of the hump.
And over some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to use softening and relaxing medicines, along with the use of the splint which we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may be used for this.  A protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it may be thought to be a dislocation, but it is an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat this with this treatment, for death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that dislocation occurs only in the hip joint and the shoulder joint, and not in other joints, which suffer slight displacement and distortion. The hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is to say, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards. Most often it is dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards. The sign of its inward dislocation is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected leg, the sound leg will be longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin. The area near the groin will be noticeably swollen because the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of outward dislocation are the opposite of these.  The sign of forward dislocation is that he extends his leg completely, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he attempts to walk, he cannot do so except forwards.
He retains his urine, his groin swells, and when he walks, he steps on his heel. The sign of backward dislocation is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly in the position of the loin. As for the reduction of these dislocations, you should observe: if the dislocation is old, has persisted for a long time with the patient, and no attempt at reduction has been made and it remains in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment.
It should not be treated. As for one whose dislocation is recent, whatever type of dislocation it is among the four types, quickly twist the joint and stretch it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, perhaps...
it will return and will not need other treatment. If it does not return like this, then you should prepare a strong, capable assistant who will stretch his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who will stretch him from above.
so that he inserts his hands under his armpits, then tightens a soft bandage around the root of the thigh, and another third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the stretching will be either from the front, from the groin towards the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
And all of them should stretch at the same time until the patient is raised from the ground and remains suspended. This type of stretching is common to the four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise, each type needs the specific treatment that I will mention.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a bandage should be applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the area under the groin.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong, capable assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and stretches it outwards with a strong pull, then it will return to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should tie the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and knees.
And the distance between each one and its partner should be four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended two fingers more than the other. Then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and his distance from the ground should be two cubits.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes strongly, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outward.
Then the patient should lie on the bench as we described for the hunchback, and the bandage should be tightened on his affected leg in particular and on his chest. Then two wooden beams are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an additional wooden beam is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
Wet rags have been wrapped around it so that the patient is not harmed. The beam should be between his thighs so that he does not be pulled downwards when extended. Then each servant pulls to his side and the physician sets the hip with his hands; if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it down as we mentioned for the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side. And if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended at once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand; meanwhile, he extends the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And it is raised above the ground, but it should be placed on something firm, just as it should also be, if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned about his lying on the bench on his face with the bandages tightened.
As we said before, and the pressing with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone. When the hip joint has been completely reduced as it should be—and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you—extend the patient's legs.
Then if you see them even, and the patient bends and straightens his leg without difficulty, know that the member has been reduced as it should be. Then bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and bind it with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days. Then untie the bandage and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the reduction has held firm, and allow the patient to walk.  If you see some laxness in it,
then re-bandage it and tighten it as you did at first, and leave it for three days also. Then untie it, and let him walk slowly on it for some days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the treatment of the reduction of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is to say backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all. The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment of all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended, if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and bend him slightly backwards; then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands, interlacing your fingers on his knee, then you press the sides of his knee strongly with your hands, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly and without constraint. Then bandage it and bind the leg to the thigh, then bind them both together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And let him only walk a little for a few days until it strengthens.
If it is impossible for you to return it by what we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be dislocated slightly, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent towards the side to which it has been dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight dislocation, it is easy to return it; this is done by gently stretching it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you seat the patient and a strong servant holds him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with the left twice, then straighten it with the left and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without violence.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg on the third occasion while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two attempts in this manner and you see the foot straight, then [well and good]; otherwise repeat the procedure on it, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and plant a strong, firmly fixed stake in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not hurt the patient, then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant should stretch the leg, either with his hands or with a bandage which he ties around the neck of the leg. Then each servant should pull in the opposite direction to his companion, while the stake remains fixed between the patient’s thighs to hold him so that his body is not pulled downwards during the stretching; then the physician should set the dislocation with his hands.
And another servant should hold the healthy leg downwards, for the dislocation will return quickly. When the dislocation has returned and you see that it is correctly returned, then apply the dressing and the splint and tie it with firm bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage to the lower part. You should avoid the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be tight upon it and hurt him. Then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day. And the patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the dislocation will not recur and be spoiled, and will not then accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, then you should use for its calming what we have previously described in another place concerning treatment and poultices, until it disappears.
Chapter thirty-four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the foot.
You should set what is dislocated with a gentle pull without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing like one walking, then you should place your foot on what is prominent from those joints, then press it down with your foot forcefully until it returns and you see that it is straight and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes the whole foot and has two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it is strengthened and you are safe from recurrence, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When something like that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it often results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except someone who is skillful in the art, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, not reckless or rash, and that he should use in the beginning only medicines which calm hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
For it is only by God's power that I hope for safety from harm with mildness of the illness and some hope appears to you in it, then return it from its place at the first, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns to what you intended, then use the treatment that calms hot swellings and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are detached fragments in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple illnesses which preceded in their places.
And strive with your utmost effort and refrain from entering the path of uncertainty, as I previously advised you, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
